Design, Fabrication, and
Characterization of Capacitive
Micromachined Ultrasound
Transducers for Laparoscopy
Nicolai Støvring (s143118)
(Author)
Erik Vilain Thomsen
(Supervisor)
Stine Løvholt Grue Pedersen
(Supervisor)
Master Thesis
Technical University of Denmark
February 1, 2020
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Ultrasound used for medical imaging has been around since the 1950s by using piezoelectric trans-
ducers. Capacitive Micromachined Ultrasound Transducers (CMUT) was introduced as an alter-
native around 25 years ago. It has several advantages over the piezoelectric transducer such as
potentially higher resolution and lower cost. The MEMS research group at the Technical Univer-
sity of Denmark (DTU) has since 2011 been researching within the ﬁeld of CMUTs. They have
fabricated CMUTs using several diﬀerent processes including a double LOCal Oxidation of Silicon
(LOCOS) process.
In this thesis new designs of a 1D linear CMUT array have been made using the results from a
simulation study over the fabrication related parameter space of CMUT cells. The new designs were
made to accommodate certain performance criteria for a laparoscopy CMUT probe. To achieve the
proposed device dimensions and performance of this probe, a novel process has to be implemented.
This process consists of etching a very deep and narrow trench and ﬁlling it with oxide. Two
batches of wafers were processed in this thesis. In the ﬁrst batch, a full process integration of the
trench etch and reﬁll with the new design was tested. The process was not successful because of
signiﬁcant stress caused by the ﬁlling of the trench among other problems.
In the second batch, designs were implemented in a double LOCOS process and afterwards
fusion bonded to a polySilicon-On-Insulator wafer made in house. The arrays were successfully
fabricated and electrically characterized where they showed the expected behaviour of the elec-
tromechanical CMUT device. It also showed potentially very good device performance though
further testing needs to be done to verify this.
==PAGE:3==
Preface
This master thesis has been submitted as the ﬁnal part for fulﬁllment for obtaining a degree in
engingeering as a Master of Science in Physics and Nanotechnology at the Technical University of
Denmark (DTU).
The project has a workload of 30 ECTS and was conducted in the period 1st of September 2019
to 1st of February 2020. All fabrication carried out in this project was done in the Clean Room
facilities overseen by DTU Nanolab.
This project is a part of the PhD project ’Capacitive Micromachined Ultrasound Transducer for
Laparoscopy’ for which Stine Løvholt Grue Pedersen is the PhD student. The project was carried
out in the MEMS group at DTU Health Tech lead by Professor Erik Vilain Thomsen. Both Stine
and Erik were supervisors on this project and I owe them a huge thanks for always being available
and excellent guidance with the project. Furthermore I would like to thank the rest of the group
for their help and discussions that has aided the project.
I would also like to express a huge gratitude to bachelor students Christoﬀer Vendelbo Sørensen
and Søren Winnerskjold Gjaldbæk, for their help with performing the measurements for electrical
characterization.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Ultrasound used for medical imaging has been around since the 1950s by using piezoelectric trans-
ducers. Capacitive Micromachined Ultrasound Transducers (CMUT) was introduced as an alter-
native around 25 years ago. It has several advantages over the piezoelectric transducer such as
potentially higher resolution and lower cost. The MEMS research group at the Technical Univer-
sity of Denmark (DTU) has since 2011 been researching within the ﬁeld of CMUTs. They have
fabricated CMUTs using several diﬀerent processes including a double LOCal Oxidation of Silicon
(LOCOS) process. This thesis deals with the fabrication of CMUTs for use in laparoscopy.
1.1
Ultrasound for medical imaging
Sound is pressure waves and ultrasound is deﬁned as sound waves having a frequency above 20
kHz, which is the limit of human hearing. Like light waves, they scatter on interfaces between
materials. Thus they can be used to image a region of interest. As pressure waves can penetrate
further into matter (depending on the wavelength), this has generated the interesting possibility to
image material in a non-invasive, non-destructive manner. These pressure waves can be generated
by a transducer, which is a device that translates energy in one domain (electrical) to energy in
another domain (mechanical/acoustic). The pressure waves, much like normal waves, are reﬂected
to some degree at boundaries depending on the acoustic impedance of the media in which the wave
travels. When meeting a boundary the amount of the wave reﬂected, RF , is given by:
RF = Z2 −Z1
Z2 + Z1
(1.1)
where Z2, Z1 are the characteristic impedances of the diﬀerent media given by:
Zi = ρici
(1.2)
where ρi is the density in the media, and ci is the speed of sound in the media [1]. As the impedance
for various kinds of tissue in the human body is diﬀerent, sound waves will be reﬂected at interfaces.
This gives the capability to diﬀerentiate diﬀerent regions based on the ’echo’ of the reﬂected signal
of a sound wave. To be able to resolve a certain feature size the wavelength should be on the order
of the given feature. Axial resolution, which is the depth resolution, can be described by [2];
ResA = n · λ
2
(1.3)
where n is the amount of pulses, or number of oscillations in the pulse, and λ = ci
f , is the wavelength
and where f is the frequency of the wave. Thus for 1 MHz frequency, 2 oscillations in a pulse and
a speed of sound in tissue of 1540 m/s, the axial resolution is 3 mm and 0.3 mm for 10 MHz
[2]. This is also the reason why high frequencies are necessary for medical imaging. For example,
1
==PAGE:7==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.1: Overview of the laparoscopy technique where the abdomen is ﬁlled with air and a small
probe is inserted through a narrow incision. Taken from [4]
the screening of embryos for potential Down’s Syndrome is a Nuchal translucency measurement.
The Nuchal has a width on the order of millimetres, and by estimating thickness in millimetres a
prognosis is given.
Lateral resolution is given by the width of the ’beam’ of pressure generated by the transducer
and can for example be adjusted by acoustic lenses.
However the waves still need to have a
wavelength that is smaller than the minimum feature size.
Another use of ultrasound in medical imaging is for Laparoscopy. A schematic overview is shown
in Figure 1.1. Recently a PhD project was funded to design and fabricate a CMUT linear array
transducer for Laparoscopy, with Stine Løvholt Grue Pedersen as the PhD student. Laparoscopy
is a technique in which one or more small incisions are made in the abdomen and a probe and/or
operating scissors are inserted through the holes. The holes are up to 1 cm in diameter and results
in less complications than for open surgery where a much larger region of the abdomen is opened.
[3] This master thesis serves to aid the PhD project.
==PAGE:8==
1.2. PIEZOELECTRIC ULTRASOUND TRANSDUCERS AND CAPACITIVE MICROMACHINED ULTRAOUND
Figure 1.2: Schematic of the CMUT cell in transmit and receive mode of operation. In transmit
mode, a pressure waves are emitted. In receive mode, pressure waves are detected and analyzed
to form an image.
1.2
Piezoelectric Ultrasound Transducers and Capacitive
Micromachined Ultraound Transducers
An ultrasound transducer is in its simplest case made up of a single ’cell’ which can be any
harmonically oscillating structure.
Most of today’s conventional transducers are piezoelectric,
which is a ceramic material that can be deformed by applying a potential. If the potential oscillates
fast enough, ultrasonic pulses will be generated.
A pressure pulse, will contain a spectrum of
frequencies at certain pressure amplitudes. The center frequency is the frequency at which the
highest pressure amplitude is emitted. The region of frequencies in which a transducer is able to
emit pressure waves without signiﬁcant loss around the center frequency is called the bandwidth
which will be further explained in Chapter 2.
Another way of generating pressure waves is by using a structure called a Capacitive Micro-
machined Ultrasound Transducer (CMUT). A very simple schematic structure is shown in Figure
1.2. A CMUT cell consist of two conducting parallel plates separated by an air/vacuum gap and
an insulating post oxide. The bottom plate, or electrode, is the substrate and can be assumed to
be completely rigid. The top plate is much thinner and forces acting upon it, will make it bend
downwards. As the two plates separated by an air gap and an insulating post oxide functions as a
capacitor, an electrical potential over the plates will result in a force acting on the system. This will
in turn make the top plate bend downwards. This is done by applying a DC bias over the plates.
If an AC signal is applied on top of the DC bias, the top plate will begin to oscillate, emitting
pressure waves. This referred to as the ’transmit mode’ operation of the CMUT. If only a DC bias
is applied and a pressure wave hits the top plate, the plate will oscillate which is converted into
an electrical signal. The signal is caused by a change in impedance, as the capacitor gap changes
(and therefore the capacitance) in time. By combining a lot of these signals from diﬀerent cells
an image can be constructed. In Chapter 2 more details will be given on how the top plate is
actuated.
To make such a structure, a Silicon wafer is processed by diﬀerent equipment in a very clean
laboratory, appropriately called a ’Clean Room’. The Clean Room is categorized based on how
many particles there is in a speciﬁc volume of air. The air is puriﬁed by ﬁlters called HEPA ﬁlters,
which clean 99.9 % of all particles. The cleanliness is very important as the structures fabricated
are very small and so even small particles can ruin the fabrication. At the Technical University of
Denmark, the Clean Room is supervised by the institute Nanolab.
By combining small cells, an ’element’ of many of these small cells can be made. Finally a lot of
elements can be put together to form an ’array’ of these elements, which make up the transducer.
The elements are electrically isolated. By using complicated electronics an image can be formed.
These parts making up a transducer is shown in Figure 1.3. The array shown in the ﬁgure is a
linear array (sometimes referred to as a 1D array) consisting of a single row of elements. A 1D
array can produce 2D images.
Comparing the performance of the piezoelectric transducer to the CMUT there are advantages
and disadvantages for both. The piezoelectric transducers produce higher pressure amplitudes than
the CMUT, as they are solid blocks of material. The CMUT however has much larger bandwidth,
==PAGE:9==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.3: Overview of the diﬀerent parts of an ultrasound transducer.
and can thus transmit and receive a higher spectrum of information in a given pulse. Due to the
Piezo being a solid block of material it also resonates harder than the CMUT and it is more diﬃcult
to impede its motion. This gives a longer pulse duration, and thus lower axial resolution. The
piezoelectric transducer also has a higher acoustic impedance than a CMUT, and several (often
expensive) matching layers are needed to use it for medical imaging. Finally the CMUT can be
fabricated using Clean Room technology which helps bring down the cost. The design of the cells,
elements and arrays are also more versatile/ﬂexible compared to conventional processing such as
sawing.
The MEMS group at DTU has over the years designed and fabricated several versions linear ar-
rays which have been mounted in handheld ultrasonic probes. These probes were named ’TABLA’,
and three versions are used as a comparison in this thesis; TABLA V, TABLA VI and TABLA
HF. TABLA V has a high bandwidth, VI has higher pressure output and HF (High Frequency)
has a higher resonance frequency. Their performance will be further elaborated in Chapter 3.
1.3
Structural overview of the CMUT cell
Over the years, diﬀerent schemes of constructing CMUT transducers have been proposed, but in
2011, Park et al. fabricated a CMUT cell using LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) [5] and
fusion bonding of two Silicon wafers. If Silicon is exposed to an Oxygen gas at high temperatures,
it will begin to grow Silicon DiOxide SiO2, which in the rest of the thesis will also be referred to
as oxide. As the oxide is grown, some of the Silicon will be consumed. If Silicon Nitride Si3N4 is
present on the surface it will act as an oxidation barrier inhibiting oxide growth in that region.
As the nitride has a ﬁnite rigidity, a structure called bird’s beak will be formed. This structure is
shown in Figure 1.4, after one LOCOS process.
In this project two consecutive LOCOS processes will be used also referred to as double LO-
COS. The double LOCOS was also used in the paper by Park et al [5], to reduce the parasitic
capacitance, CP . Parasitic capacitance is the contribution to the overall capacitance from the
insulating dielectric region. To detect a signal in receive mode, the relative change in capacitance
due to top plate bending is measured. Thus the higher the constant parasitic capacitance, the
lower the relative change will be. In Figure 1.5 a schematic of the CMUT structure after double
LOCOS process is shown. Names for diﬀerent parts of the double LOCOS is also shown in the
ﬁgure. The ’Si-Bump’ is the Silicon bump created during the ﬁrst LOCOS process. The height of
the Si-bump will be denoted hB. The ’Top Plate’ is the plate that is actuated by a voltage and is
==PAGE:10==
1.3. STRUCTURAL OVERVIEW OF THE CMUT CELL
5
Figure 1.4: Figure showing the ’Bird’s beak structure after one LOCOS step.
Figure 1.5: Figure showing an overview of the double LOCOS CMUT cell, with a fusion bonded
top plate, along with the names of the diﬀerent strucutres/regions.
in this project made out of poly-cystalline Silicon (polySilicon), and its thickness denoted h. The
substrate is made of single crystalline Silicon. The post-oxide, with thickness, tpost is the oxide
which isolate individual cells. It also serves as the bonding interface the top plate is bonded to.
The pad oxide is an oxide layer that serves to relieve stresses from the nitride layer, with thickness
tni. The pad oxide also called isolation oxide because it prevents short circuit if the top plate
collapses on the bump. It is called pad oxide during the ﬁrst LOCOS, and isolation oxide during
the second LOCOS, but both will be denoted as tpad. If one puts a large DC bias on the CMUT
structure the top plate will be pulled down and hit the Si-bump. The top plate will simply collapse
unto the Si-bump at a certain bias threshold. This is called ’pull-in’, which will also be described
further in Chapter 2. The air gap, g between between the bump and the top plate is simply called
the ’Gap’. The slopes of the Si-bump and the post oxide are the regions between horizontally ﬂat
surface.
With a better overview of the double LOCOS structure, more parameters can be deﬁned; The
distance between the center of one element to the center of the next element is called the ’Pitch’
and will be denoted dp. The spacing separating two elements is called the ’Kerf’, dk. Also relevant
is the smallest distance between the perimeter of a cell to the edge of the element, which will be
called ’Distance to trench’ for reasons that will be clear later, denoted by dt. This is shown in
Figure 1.6. The outer circles deﬁnes the regions of the cells, and are also referred to as ’Cavity’,
because they deﬁne the region of the air cavity. The radius of the cell/cavity is deﬁned RC or
simply R if there is no ambiguity. The inner circle deﬁnes the Si-bump and will be denoted as RB.
==PAGE:11==
6
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.6: Figure showing the deﬁnition of pitch, kerf and distance to trench.
1.4
State of the art
As stated above this project served to aid the PhD project for creating a CMUT for laparoscopy.
For a laparoscopic probe, the width of the transducer should be no more than 8 mm, and the
length no more than 35 mm. The thickness of the mounted transducers should preferably be as
thin as possible.
A new way to structure the CMUT, called Deposit, Remove, Etch, Multistep (DREM) has
recently been published[6]. DREM allows for etching very deep and thin trenches in Silicon, which
will be described further in Chapter 2. This in turn allows the elements to be separated in the
substrate instead of on the top plate. This makes it possible to package the CMUT array smaller as
the CMUT array is made thinner. As the DREM is used for etching around 100 µm deep trenches,
this is also going to be the thickness of the transducer before packaging. The reason is that for the
elements to be electrically separated by the trench, the rest of the substrate has to be removed.
The initial wafer thickness is 500 µm, so with the new method, the array is one ﬁfth of the current
array thickness.
Currently the arrays are wire-bonded for electrical connection. This technique involves con-
necting small wires from an element to an electrical pad on a printed curcuit board. This small
wire is both fragile, and extends some millimetres into the air. If the elements are separated in the
substrate, ﬂip-chip bonding can be used instead. In ﬂip-chip bonding, the electrical connections
to pads are directly bonded to a printed circuit board, eliminating the need for the small wires.
The etched trench need to be ﬁlled with an insulating material for this to work, as the elements
would just fall apart otherwise. Previously the substrate element separation by etching has been
demonstrated [7]. The DREM etch has also already been realized in a CMUT array by the MEMS
group by bonding to an insulating bottom substrate, much like the structure described in [7]. The
same group has also demonstrated reﬁlling of the etched trenches [8]. These trenches were quite
wide and were willed with PDMS, which also resulted in a PDMS coated top plate. Complete
ﬁlling of the DREM etched trench by oxidation has not yet been realized. A schematic of how the
trench etch could be ﬁlled by oxidation is shown in Figure 1.7.
The transducer should have a center frequency around 7.5 MHz and ideally its bandwidth
should be between a low limit of 2 and a high limit 10 MHz. Higher bandwidth and operating
frequency usually results in higher image quality. The low limit is set because the probe might
have to be used in Contrast-Enhanced Ultrasound Imaging (CEUS). This technique involves the
use of injecting a medium containing air micro bubbles to enhance contrast.[9] The bubbles will
burst if too high frequencies are used. This undesirable as it might hurt the patient.
==PAGE:12==
1.4. STATE OF THE ART
7
Figure 1.7: Figure showing how the trench is potentially reﬁlled by oxidation.
It is important to design the CMUT so that it matches the desired speciﬁcations. However it
is also important to have a fabrication process with high yield. Therefore it might make sense in a
fabrication point of view to make very ’safe’ structures, but the safe structure might not be viable
in actual use. A focus in this thesis was to try to accommodate both. In the design phase this was
incorporated by ﬁrst optimizing for performance. Then some of these parameters were adjusted
towards more ’safe’ fabrication and processing.
Results from an expansive simulation study was analyzed to aid the design phase of the project.
The simulations were carried out in Onscale(also known as PZFlex), which is a Finite Element
software that is very capable within the ﬁeld of ultrasound transducers. The simulations were
programmed and carried out by Postdoc Mathias Engholm.
Athena Silvaco, a process simulation software, was used to aid the processing phase of the
project. Due to licensing issues these could not be performed before processing had started, and
was thus also aimed at aiding future design and processing.
==PAGE:13==
8
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.5
Aims of the thesis
The aim of this thesis is to:
• Study the simulations made by Post Doc Mathias Engholm in Onscale in depth
• Use the conclusion of the simulations to design a new set of masks for new linear array designs
for laparoscopy
• Search for ways to incorporate a ﬁlled DREM trench into a linear CMUT array, and identify
possible obstacles when incorporating them
• Incorporate in the mask design, a study for multiple paths to incorporate a DREM trench
with ﬁlling in the fabrication process
• Incorporate the DREM trench with ﬁlling into the double-LOCOS process
• Fabricate a double-LOCOS CMUT with the cell parameters found in the simulations
• Characterize the CMUT elements and compare them with simulations in Onscale.
1.6
outline of thesis structure
Generally there will be a short introduction of- and motivation for the chapter, at the beginning of
every chapter. There also be a sub conclusion (summary) in every chapter summarizing the main
points. Choices and reﬂections are discussed throughout the report for a better ﬂow, instead of
dedicating a chapter to discussion.
• Chapter 2 - Theory will present theory that is relevant for the rest of the thesis.
• Chapter 3 - Simulation of Design Parameters will go through and analyze the OnScale
simulations performed by Post Doc Mathias Engholm. Based on the analysis, parameters for
the CMUT will be chosen.
• Chapter 4 - Design of Mask will go through the process ﬂow for fabrication, as well as
describe the new mask design made in this project.
• Chapter 5 - Process simulation using Athena Silvaco concerns process simulation on
the viability of the CMUT structure designed in this project, as well as searching for ways
to improve upon it.
• Chapter 6 - Fabrication; Batch 1 will go through the processing on the ﬁrst batch of
wafers. The ﬁrst batch was a trial in full process integration with the new ﬁlled DREM
etched trenches. In this chapter, the focus was also to describe the motivation behind the
diﬀerent steps in the process and how they work.
• Chapter 7 - Fabrication; Batch 2 will go through the processing on the second batch
of wafers. The second batch was a conventional double LOCOS without the DREM process
with the focus on successful fabrication to be able to electrically characterize the arrays.
The focus for this batch is a more thorough documentation of every step to try and detect
possible errors, which might aﬀect performance and fabrication success.
• Chapter 8 - Electrical Characterization will present and discuss the electrical charac-
terization of the CMUTs.
• Chapter 9 - Conclusion and Outlook will conclude the work of the thesis and give an
outline for future work that could be done.
==PAGE:14==
Chapter 2
Theory
In the Theory chapter several sections will be covered. First oﬀthe theory behind the simulation in
PZFlex which is a Finite Element (FEM) simulation software tool designed for wave propagation
calculations.
It has electromechanical materials incorporated - making it well suited for time-
domain analysis of CMUT behaviour. Secondly, some geometrical theory and considerations will
be elaborated to be able to have variety of options when designing CMUT elements, employing
circular cells. Thirdly the static electromechanical view of the CMUT will be brieﬂy explained.
The fourth part will contain a short introduction to the electrodynamic behavior of the CMUT
in an equivalent circuit model. Finally some theory about the oxidation of Silicon and the new
dry etching technique, DREM, will be explained. The rest of the theory for the diﬀerent Silicon
processing will be covered during the fabrication chapter to achieve a better ﬂow of the thesis. The
parts covered in this chapter will serve to enable the discussion much of the discussion throughout
the thesis.
2.1
PZFlex/Onscale simulation of plate thickness and cell
radius
The PZFLex and Onscale software is the same, but to diﬀerentiate the models used, they will be
described as being diﬀerent pieces of software.
The simulation performed in Onscale by Post Doc Mathias Engholm [10], will lay the founda-
tion of how the parameters for the design of the CMUT arrays can be chosen. Initial simulations
were done in the PZFlex software because it is designed speciﬁcally for problems related to ultra-
sonic transduction behaviour in the time-domain. Here it was shown that the model was in good
correspondence with both analytical and empirical results. The diﬀerence in the electromechanical
part of the simulation were within 3 percent. [10]
The model was then taken to Onscale. This is a cloud computing platform available so a large
study can be done in a smaller time frame. Onscale makes it possible to do a sweep of a large
parameter space with time domain analysis in just a few hours. The model was altered from being
a row of element with a periodic boundary condition to a unit cell of a hexagonal array with
periodic boundary conditions. This meant that it could no longer capture cross talk.
The CMUT unit cell is built up of a silicon substrate layer, an isolation oxide on top of this,
and a cavity in part of the silicon oxide.
The cavity is sealed by a Silicon plate on which an
Aluminum layer is on top. This is similar to a single LOCOS CMUT, with the diﬀerence that
the edges of the cavity are more sharp. The cavity air gap is adjusted so that the pull-in voltage
remains ﬁxed at 200 V. The signal input into the cells is a Unipolar Square wave to ensure a wide
frequency spectrum input signal. The unit cell have a constant area of 1 cm2 and a ﬁxed distance
of 5 µm between cells. Symmetric boundary conditions have been applied in the x-y-axis so only a
single unit cell of the hexagonal array has to be simulated. This results in faster simulation. The
substrate has a solid bottom boundary - simulating the eﬀect at the Silicon-Air interface. The
wave transmitted propagates upwards until it meets a boundary with the boundary condition of a
perfectly matched layer (PML). The PML dampens the way and simulates the wave propagating
9
==PAGE:15==
10
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
Figure 2.1: Screenshot of the OnScale simulation at a single time step. The top part shows the
velocity ath the center of the. The bottom shows the pressure and the graph shows the pressure
just below the top of the domain.
out into an inﬁnite medium. Thus the wave generated is a plane wave as seen produced from an
inﬁnite array of cells. The simulation domain is shown in Figure 2.1
A DC bias is applied to the CMUT unit cell (80 % of vP I), and then a square wave AC-pulse is
applied to generate the signal. The signal ﬁrst propagates through silicone, that forms an acoustic
window to the water domain. The signal is measured a small distance from the top of the cell in a
transmit simulation, where the response from a CMUT is studied. This distance is approximately
300 um away from the top of the simulated domain. A similar signal is generated from the top
domain to simulate an incoming signal to ﬁnd the response from the CMUT when receiving the
signal.
For the transmit and receive mode simulations plate thickness were varied from 0.5 um to 15
um and the radius of the cells were varied from 10 to 80 um.
The simulation extracts the driving signal (AC potential) on the CMUT (drive), the velocity
in the center of the plate(vel), the pressure amplitude at 300 um away from the CMUT(aprs).
This is then transformed into the frequency domain using a Hann window and Fast Fourier
Transform of the data. This gives the frequency spectra of the pressure amplitude, the driving
signal, plate velocity, the impedance and the output pressure divided by the driving signal. These
are used to calculate the normalised signal output on a logscale. The peak amplitude is found and
from there, ﬁnd the -3dB points on the spectrum. The usual convention is to ﬁnd the -6dB points
when measuring on actual probes, but these measurements are actually a combination of both
the transmit and receive characteristics. These points are found relative to the peak amplitude
frequency, fp. The peak amplitude frequency is also found, which in most cases for CMUTs is
diﬀerent from the center frequency.
The center frequency, fc, is found as:
fc = 1
2(BWH + BWL)
(2.1)
==PAGE:16==
2.2. FILL FACTOR, PITCH AND CELL SEPARATION
11
where BWH and BWL are the -x dB high and low frequency limit relative to the peak amplitude.
The BandWidth(BW) is found as:
BW = 2BWH −BWL
BWH + BWL
(2.2)
If the center frequency is diﬀerent than the peak amplitude frequency the signal is asymmetrical.
It is important to have a high bandwidth in ultrasonic transducers, as that gives a higher resolution
in both the transmitted and received signal. The reason is that the signal contains more information
of the medium with a higher frequency spectrum. The signal needs to be higher than the noise
ﬂoor of course, so the total pressure output is also important. Another descriptor for the CMUT
performance could thus be the integral of the pressure output over the range of the bandwidth:
ICMUT =
Z BWH
BWL
p(f)df
(2.3)
where p(f) is the pressure amplitude frequency spectrum. This will be called the CMUT informa-
tion. This can describe both the bandwidth and the pressure in the same value. The descriptor
will favor frequency spectra which are quadratic in shape rather than rectangular. This means that
it weights signal with medium pressure output and BW, higher than one that is high in pressure
output and low in BW or vice versa.
2.2
Fill factor, pitch and cell separation
Fill factor is important when designing the elements, because it determines the pressure output of
the probe. The challenge is to ﬁt as many cells into an element given the constraints of the pitch
desired. The pitch depends on the peak frequency in the system.
The geometry used for the cells were circles in this project. Circles can be packed into a square
in diﬀerent ways. If they are packed in a rectangular pattern the packing fraction, or equivalently,
the ﬁll factor is given by the following consideration:
The unit cell in rectangular packing of circle consists of a square with side length a, and a
quarter of a circle inside this square with radius a. then the ﬁll factor, F, is the ratio between the
area of the square and the area of the circle quarter:
FR = πa2
4a2 = π
4 ≈0.785
(2.4)
For hexagonal packing, the unit cell can still be a rectangle, but to see the side length of the
rectangle, a hexagon is ﬁrst drawn to the center of each circle in a hexagonal grid as seen in Figure
2.2.
Here can be seen that the hexagon can be split into triangles. From simply looking at the
situation it can be seen that ahex = 2R. From Pythagoras can be seen that the other cathete, b,
of the hexagon is

which is the optimal packing for circles if there are no constraints in the packing area.
The cells cannot be cut through the middle however, and so they are restricted to whatever
shape and size of element they are ﬁt into. The shape of the element could be made into a hexagonal
structure itself, which gives a larger ﬁll factor overall than if packed into rectangular elements. The
design of mask is a little more cumbersome ﬁr hexagonal structures however, and several process
steps might be more diﬃcult to control on a Silicon wafer. Meanwhile, even though the individual
elements are hexagonal the overall array is a rectangle. Therefore this section is going to explore
the diﬀerent kinds of packing. Packing is done with the constraint that the circles cannot overlap.
There are several ways to pack spheres into a rectangle, in fact there are proven optimal
ways[11]. Here rectangular and hexagonal ’zigzag/armchair’ edge (see Figure 2.2) packing is going
to be elaborated.
If the circles are packed rectangularly, then the distance between two circles, with radius r, in
both the x- and y-direction will be 2R. For a given rectangle with side length X in the x-direction
and side length Y in the y-direction the number of circles in either direction is going to be
nx = X
2R,
(2.7)

2R
(2.8)

where the second term on the RHS in equation 2.10 stems from the fact that the edge of the
rectangle is only R away from the ﬁrst circle and the plus one comes into play if the rectangle can
only ﬁt one circle in that direction. Given the nature of hexagonal packing, there exist two cases
for the packing. If nx is an even number the packing can be alternated between nx and nx −1
number of circles in the y-direction. nx and ny is rounded upwards to the nearest integer.
==PAGE:18==
2.3. THEORY ABOUT PLATES, CAPACITORS AND OTHER GENERAL CMUT THEORY13
Using this, the amount of cells of an element or array can now be calculated in the x- and
y-direction, given the pitch and and dimensions of the ﬁnal probe. Using a script, the best packing
method that gives the highest ﬁll factor can be found for a given rectangle, which will also be done
later when choosing parameters.
As stated above, the pitch will largely determine the dimensions of the elements. The reason
is that ultrasonic transducers, phased areas are commonly used to steer and focus beams. Phased
arrays, are simply an array in which the activation (actuation) of the elements is not done uniformly
in time. This is done by introducing a small delay in the signal sent to the diﬀerent elements. By
doing this, constructive interference is created and thus the direction and focus of the ultrasound
beam can be adjusted.
This constructive interference will also be created in other directions
however. There will be so-called lobes at speciﬁc angles where the intensity is stronger compared
to other directions. There will be a main lobe, often directly perpendicular to the transducer which
is the strongest, but also signiﬁcant side lobes might be created depending on the transducer. In a
linear phased array there will be side lobes called grating lobes. These lobes will be signiﬁcant if
the pitch of the elements, p, is larger than the wavelength in the medium, λ. These will interfere
with image formation because there will be a signal contribution from a place, other than the one
actually looked at and avoiding them is thus desired. These grating lobes will be created according
to[12]:
sin(θk) = kλ
p −sin(θ)
(2.11)
where, θk is the angle at which the grating lobe will be formed, k is an integer, λ is the
wavelength in the medium, p is the pitch of the elements and θ is the angle of which the main lobe
is steered towards. To be sure no grating lobe exists in the entirety of the image which could be
from 0 to ±90◦for the ﬁrst grating lobe k = 1:
sin(90) < 1λ
p −sin(90) =⇒p ≤λ
2
(2.12)
And more conservative; if the image area is only within ±30◦
sin(30) < 1λ
p −sin(30) =⇒p ≤λ
(2.13)
Where it can be seen that the element pitch should be between λ
2 ≤p ≤λ. Speed of sound in
water at room temperature is around 1483 m
s which for a 10 MHz pulse gives a wavelength of:
λ =
1483 m
s
10 · 106Hz ≈145µm
(2.14)
Wavelengths around 1-10 MHz are relevant in this project giving wavelengths on the order of
100-1000 µm. The pitch should then be on the order of 50-500 µm which results in few cells per
element width. As shall be seen in Chapter 5 this can have a large impact in the design and
fabrication of the CMUT, as there needs to be certain separation between cells and elements.
In this project the pitch was not tested, but as it aﬀects the design it was included from the
beginning.

such as the cell radius and plate thickness is important. This section serves to provide the reader
with this framework.

2.5
Equivalent Circuit - CMUT Electrodynamics
To later being able to describe the electrical characterization of the CMUT better, a brief overview
of the CMUT electrodynamics is given here.
In an electrical circuit, A potential is applied to drive a current through impedances. Three
diﬀerent kinds of impedance components exist. A resistor which has the impedance ZR = R1
(with the subscript 1 so as not to be confused with the radius of the cell).
A capacitor with
impedance ZC =
1
jωC where ω is the frequency the system is driven at. An inductor with impedance
ZL = jωL. Any domain(mechanical, acoustic etc) can be converted into an equivalent circuit by
a proper transformation, by considering the generalized potential, ’eﬀort’, the generalized Charge
’displacement’ and the generalized current ’ﬂow’. The impedance components of the CMUT can
mathematically be converted into an equivalent circuit where;
m = L

The oﬀdiagonal elements represent the electromechanical coupling. The form of the coupling
shows, that if the derivative of the capacitance, or the potential bias voltage increases so does the
coupling. As the derivative increases as the plate is pulled down the plate should be pulled down
as much as possible. This means that the CMUT should be actuated close to pull-in. The ﬁrst
diagonal element represent the blocked electrical (impedance) circuit meaning the velocity is not
changing. The second diagonal describes the short-circuited mechanical (impedance) circuit, when
the voltage is constant and no current is running.
The total impedance as a function of frequency is plotted in Figure 2.4. Initially the CMUT
functions as a capacitor and the impedance decreases with increasing frequency. Here the phase
between voltage is shifted -90◦out of phase to the current.
This is because the potential over
a capacitor lags behind the current supplied to build up charge.
At the resonance frequency,
the spring and mass impedance become 180◦out of phase and equal in magnitude reducing the
impedance to 0 in a lossless environment. This is equal to writing |Zm,a| = 0 =⇒ωres =
q
k
m
After this point the phase shifts to +90◦as the impedance is now dominated by the mass. An
inductor resists change in current and so the current lags behind the potential.
At the anti
resonance the mechanical and electrical impedance become equal in magnitude and 180 ◦out of
phase giving an inﬁnite impedance. This is equal to writing

2.6
Cleanroom stuﬀ- especially, DREM, oxidation and bond-
ing
This section will deal with presenting an overview of oxidation of Silicon and of the principle behind
the DREM etching technique. This will aid in understanding process simulation and design of
thicknesses of the Si-Bump and post oxide. The theory of oxidation will take basis in [16].
Silicon Dioxide can be grown in two gas chemistries, either dry with O2 or wet with H2O. The
chemical process taking place in is:
Si(solid) + O2(gas) →SiO2(solid)
(2.58)
Si(solid) + 2 H2O(gas) →SiO2(solid) + H2
(2.59)
As the oxide has a higher volume than Silicon itself, the oxide grown would like to expand. A
way to estimate the the oxide expands is by the ratio of the volume of Silicon to the oxide. The
molar mass of Si and oxide are 28.09 g mol−1 and 60.08 g mol−1 respectively. The density of Si is
2,33 g cm−3 and SiO2 is 2,65 g cm−3. the ratio of molar volumes is then:
VSi
VSiO2
=
28.09 gmol−1
2.32 gcm−3
60.08 gmol−1
2.21 gcm−3
= 0.44
(2.60)
which is also the value used by the process simulation software Athena [17]. The value used by
[16] is 1/(1.3)3 = 0.4545 where it is stated that the SiO2 would like to expand 1.3 times the length
of a unit Silicon in all directions.
Depending on the sources of densities, diﬀerent values and
thus diﬀerent ratios are found. This can have quite high implications on the designed bump and
post oxide. Furthermore the oxide is grown on diﬀerent geometries and at diﬀerent temperatures,
==PAGE:25==
20
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
which in turn will aﬀect how the oxide can expand. The better option(if the characterization tool
is reliable), is to measure the heights along the fabrication to adjust process values. The value 0.44
was used for the ratio in the calculations in this thesis.
The way an oxide is grown conceptually, is that the available oxygen in the gas diﬀuses to the
Silicon surface and reacts with it. By considering the ﬂuxes at the oxide/air interface, through the
oxide and the Oxide/Silicon interface:
Fox,air = F1 = hg(C′ −CO)
HkT
= h(C′ −CO)
(2.61)
Fdif,ox = F2 = −D∂C
∂x = dCO −CI
xO
(2.62)
Fox,Si = F3 = ksCI
(2.63)
, hg is the mass transfer coeﬃcient, C′ is the oxidant concentration in equilibrium with the bulk gas
pressure, CO is the concentration of oxide just below the oxide surface H is Henry’s constant, k is
Boltzmann’s constant and T is the temperature. h is assumed to be large due to high temperature
and large mass transfer. D is the diﬀusion constant, ks the reaction rate constant, xO the oxide
thickness and CI the Silicon interface concentration. Equating these ﬂuxes give:
CI =
C′
1 + ks
h + ksxO
D
≈
C′
1 + ksxO
D
(2.64)
If the oxide is very thin, the process is reaction limited as there will always be available oxygen
molecules - CI ≈C′. If the oxide is thick the oxidation will be diﬀusion limited, as the reaction
happens quicker than Oxygen can diﬀuse through - CI ≈0 the oxide.
Using F3 and the equation for CI, dividing by the number of oxidant molecules per unit volume,
N:
dxO

= F3

(2.65)
Solving it with limits xO and xi, the initial thickness of the oxide gives the oxidation time to
grow xO:
x2

+ xO −xi

(2.66)

(2.67)

Where B is called the parabolic rate constant and A is called the linear rate constant. τ can
be understood as the time it has taken to grow an initial oxide, and can thus be used to correct
for oxidation time. The rate constant are given by an Arrhenius expression:
B, A = KB,A exp −EB,A
kT
(2.69)
Where K is some constant and E is an activation energy. From this can be seen, that the
oxidation is very temperature dependent.
From this and the volume expansion ratio, the oxidation time, bump height and post oxide
thickness can now be estimated.
To make matters more complicated, D, ks, C′ and the viscosity of the oxide µ are all stress
dependent, with a similar Arrhenius expression describing them, where the activation energies by a
==PAGE:26==
2.6. CLEANROOM STUFF - ESPECIALLY, DREM, OXIDATION AND BONDING
21
Figure 2.5: Figure showing the normalized oxide thickness as a function of radius of curvature
of microstructures. It shows that at lower temperaturs, 2D geometry has big implications on the
oxidation rate. Figure taken from [18]
ﬁtting parameter and the stresses in the system. It has been found experimentally that the radius
of curvature of 2D structures greatly impact the oxidation rate as shown in Figure 2.5 [18]
In the process an opening in the Silicon will be made by the DREM etch, which is a nearly
vertical etch. Thus the edges of the trench will likely aﬀect the post oxide surface around the
trench opening.
To electrically separate the elements in a linear CMUT array, a top plate etch has usually been
employed. The high bias potential is applied on the electrically insulated element, and the ground
is connected to all elements. The reverse cannot be done, as that would bias all elements. This
creates a at least two problems. As described above, the higher the bias the more sensitive to small
perturbations the CMUT is. Thus the array must be electrically shielded by a metal foil to avoid
noise. Secondly the high bias is applied closer to the patient, which could result in accidents.
To reverse the situation the elements must be separated in the substrate. This is done by
etching a deep trench and removing the backside of the substrate until hitting these trenches.
This however gives some fabrication related issues. The trench much be ﬁlled, or when thinning
the backside of the wafer must be bonded to another substrate and afterwards bonded to a third
substrate. Otherwise the array will fall apart. The top plate separation can be very well deﬁned
==PAGE:27==
22
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
as it is shallow etch. The substrate element deﬁnition using a deep trench will possibly take up
more space especially if it is oxidized.
To even make the trench deep enough without having a very large opening has been a problem.
To make deep anisotropic etches, so-called ’dry’ etching has been used. in dry etching a plasma
is generated by an AC ﬁeld. The plasma contains a lot of free radicals. These radicals are then
accelerated unto the substrate-to-be-etched on which they adsorb or sputter of material. the more
chemical based the etch is, the more isotropic it will be. As they adsorb and if the chemistry is
right, they will form volatile products with the surface of the substrate which are then desorbed
and pumped away.
In Silicon this principle can be used to both deposit a thin layer of polymer using a C4F8 gas
chemistry. Using a SF6 gas chemistry will result in an isotropic etch. Cycling between these two
steps with correct intervals will result in polymer being deposited on sidewalls and the horizontal
surface to be etched. This is called the ’Bosch process’. This works well in creating anisotropic
structures, but due to Reactive Ion Etching (RIE) lag and side wall corrosion, very high-aspect
ratio structures can be diﬃcult. RIE lag is the fact that the process slows down as the aspect ratio
increases while the etch rate. This also causes the trenches of wider openings to be etched faster
than narrow openings.
In a recent paper[6], an improved Bosch process was described. Another step is injected into
the Bosch cycle; An Argon sputter step. This is a more directed etch on horizontal surface, and it
can help alleviate the problems in a normal Bosch process, if the steps are carefully tuned. This 3-
step process is called Deposit, Remove, Etch, Multistep (DREM). In the paper, they demonstrated
aspect ratios of around 50. For a 2.5 µm trench opening this can give a trench of at least 125 µm.
They also demonstrated Sausage-like pillars, in which a longer isotropic etch was introduced every
10 DREM cycles.
2.7
Summary
In this chapter, a brief overview was given on several subjects. The Onscale simulations made by
Post Doc Mathias Engholm was introduced. A short introduction to bandwidth, element pitch
and why they are important was also given. The geometry of packing circles was also described to
understand how the mask design should be made. CMUT electrostatics- and dynamics were also
described to understand how the CMUT cells should be designed. Finally Oxidation and DREM to
essential processes in the fabrication of the double LOCOS structure in this thesis was explained.
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 3
Simulation of design parameters
There are several diﬀerent design parameters to tune to get a CMUT with the desired speciﬁcations.
The parameters investigated in this thesis were the thickness of the top plate, h, the radius of the
CMUT cell, R. As was seen in Chapter 2 and also will be seen in this section, a lot of other
parameters depend on the choices of these. Based on a study initially performed by Post Doc
M. Engholm from the MEMS group at DTU, on how h and R aﬀected CMUT performance are
discussed and chosen. This chapter is organized the following way; First there will be a run-through
of the output characteristics of the CMUT, the transmit and then the receive characteristics will be
descirbed shortly. Finally they will be combined to give an input-output characteristic, as would
be the case when using the CMUT as a ultrasound probe. The output-input mode is referred to
as ’pulse-echo’.

CMUT pattern, with ﬁxed pull-in potential and electrode area so pressure and bandwidth, among

3.1. TRANSMIT SIMULATIONS

Figure 3.3: Bandwidth plotted versus radius of CMUT cells and plate thickness, for the CMUT in

Figure 3.4: Pressure output plotted versus radius of CMUT cells and plate thickness, for the CMUT
in transmit mode of operation. The points at which the TABLA probes should lie according to the
simulation, have also been plotted for reference. Contour lines for the resonance frequency have

From Figure 3.4 it can be seen that increasing the radius gives a lower pressure output, while
increasing the plate thickness seems to increase the pressure output. This also makes intuitive
sense, the thicker the plate, the more momentum it can provide, thereby giving a higher pressure
output. Conversely the larger the plate, the less rigid it becomes, thereby providing lower force
per area, i.e. pressure.
==PAGE:31==
26
CHAPTER 3. SIMULATION OF DESIGN PARAMETERS
Figure 3.5: ’CMUT Information’ according to Equation 2.3 plotted versus radius of CMUT cells
and plate thickness, for the CMUT in transmit mode of operation. The points at which the TABLA
probes should lie according to the simulation, have also been plotted for reference. Contour lines
for the resonance frequency have been plotted.
Looking at Figure 3.3 can be seen that the bandwidth @-3dB of the CMUT increases with
increasing cell radius and decreases with increasing plate thickness. From the contour lines can
also be seen that at lower resonance frequencies, the bandwidth is generally higher. This may be
due to how bandwidth is deﬁned mathematically in Equation 2.2, where the bandwidth is conﬁned
within a certain region.
Noting that this is the inverse relationship compared to the pressure
output, a compromise must be made in designing the CMUT. One has to choose between good
bandwidth or high pressure output, or somewhere in between.
To try and ﬁnd a good compromise, the descriptor for the ’information’ of the CMUT as
described in Equation 2.3 was also considered. This is plotted in Figure 3.5. Again, it increases
with plate thickness, and decreases with cell radius. The maximum region has shifted a little to
the left when comparing the information to the pressure output.
These relations are also shown in Figure 3.6, where either plate thickness or cell radius has
been kept constant. for the plots to the left R = 30µm and for the plots to the right h = 6µm.
The pressure and information are plotted in a log scale to show diﬀerent regimes. Interestingly,
the pressure and energy increases a lot with plate thickness initially, but the the decrease levels oﬀ.
Thus the parameters seem to have two regimes when plotted against plate thickness. The reverse
is true for the bandwidth which quickly drops to around 20 % and levels oﬀas a function of plate
thickness. When plotted against radius, there does not seem to be two regimes. The bandwidth
increases with increasing cell radius and information/pressure decreases.
It is often desired to work around a certain resonance frequency, as deviations away from this
frequency will result in a lower signal amplitude as shown in Figure 3.7. Usually, the resonance
frequency is also called the center frequency, denoting that it lies in the middle of the bandwidth.
For CMUT’s, that is not accurate. This is because the CMUT bandwidth is asymmetric with a
bigger decrease in lower frequencies than at higher frequencies. A typical spectrum is shown in
Figure 3.7.
A center frequency of around 7.5 MHz was desired as an initial estimate as that would give
lower and upper bandwidth bounds that would make it able to work within the desired region of
frequencies. This criteria was provided by BK Medical as described in Chapter 1.
As can be seen in Figure 3.1-3.5, the diﬀerent parameters can also be plotted while going along
one of the contour lines. Thus the bandwidth and pressure output can be plotted as a function

meet certain design requirements according to Equations 2.26 and 2.27. This makes a constant
resonance frequency plot a relevant method of visualising the performance characteristics. This
also means that the plots become more abstract as there is now an underlying change in plate
thickness when plotting against radius. As shown in Figure 3.6, the BW, Pressure output, and
Information dependencies of h are opposite to their dependency R.
In the following the resonance frequency will be kept constant at 7.5 MHz unless otherwise
speciﬁed.
A quick way to gain an overview is to do a matrix plot, in which every parameter is plotted
against one another as shown in Figure 3.8. The plots in the ﬁgure are going to be speciﬁed as
(row,column) In the diagonal is shown histograms of the amount of counts in a given bin. There
are 15 bins over the range of the speciﬁc data. In plot (2,1) is shown the plate thickness plotted
against the radius while the resonance frequency is kept constant at 7.5 MHz. This is basically the
same as the 7.5 MHz contour line in the surface color plots.
In plot (3,1) the Bandwidth is plotted versus radius. It shows that the Bandwidth is linearly
decreasing with increasing radius. This is in contrast with what is shown in Figure 3.6. This is
due to the fact that there is an underlying increase in plate thickness. In plot (3,2) Bandwidth
is plotted against the plate thickness. Here can be seen a more logarithmic behavior than in plot
(3,1). It is however still decreasing, which ﬁts into what was seen in Figure 3.6. Thus it can be
seen that the decrease in bandwidth as a function of radius at constant resonance frequency is
actually due to the plate thickness increase. This shows that if the designer of the CMUT cell
wants to have a ﬁxed resonance frequency, the lower the cell radius and therefore plate thickness,
the higher the bandwidth.
Looking at the pressure output as a function of radius (plot 4,1) it seems to increase linearly,
which again contrast the behaviour shown in Figure 3.6. As a function of plate thickness in plot
(4,2) the pressure also seems to increases linearly. In Figure 3.6 the increase was logarithmic.
Combining the diﬀerent observations, the performance characteristics seems to be most dependent
on the plate thickness.
Another interesting plot is pressure as a function of bandwidth (4,3), where the pressure output
can be seen to decrease with increasing bandwidth, showing directly that high bandwidth and high
pressure cannot be attained simultaneously.
Finally, comparing the information of the CMUT to cell radius and plate thickness in plot (5,1)
and (5,2), a decrease is shown. It is a similar decrease in both cases. The information increases
with an increase in bandwidth as the integration area becomes larger. The information decreases
with increasing pressure however. As the pressure increases, one would expect the information to
increase as well, as the integration should become larger. This is probably due to an underlying
dependency on the bandwidth. The bandwidth decreases with increasing pressure, which in turn
causes the integration area to become smaller. Thus in the case of how the information is deﬁned,
the bandwidth is more signiﬁcant than the pressure.
As there is a bandwidth/pressure tradeoﬀ, the bandwidth and frequency spectrum of the CMUT
will now be studied further.
In addition to the deﬁnition of bandwidth in Equation 2.2 there are more deﬁnitions. As this
deﬁnition makes the bandwidth bound within 0 and 200. This means that a signiﬁcant increase in
the upper bandwidth limit does not give that much value if the value is already high compared to
the lower limit. The other deﬁnition is the bandwidth ratio which is deﬁned as:
BWR = BWH
BWL
(3.1)
Which gives more value to high upper limits and is unbounded. The two deﬁnitions are plotted
in Figure 3.9 with an upper bandwidth limit from 0 to 40, and a lower one from 0 to 5. Here are
seen that as the high bandwidth limit becomes very high compared to the lower one, the slope
becomes smaller.
In the other case, the slope stays the same, if the lower bandwidth limit is
constant.
This is also the case for the simulated values of the CMUT parameters as seen in Figure 3.10.
The yellow line shows the bandwidth ratio, BWR and the orange line shows the original bandwidth
deﬁnition BW. Comparing the two lines, it is evident, that the bandwidth ratio decreases faster,

and thus might be more suited to describe the bandwidth for CMUT’s. The blue line shows the
bandwidth if the resonance frequency is used instead of the center frequency. The discrepancy is
due to the asymmetry in the frequency spectrum mentioned earlier. As the cell radius increases
(and therefore also the plate thickness), the spectrum becomes more symmetric.
These considerations of how the value of the bandwidth is displayed, and as such, also how
the requirements for future devices will be given, shows that simply stating a center frequency
and bandwidth might not be optimal for CMUT’s. Therefore, another method might be to look
at the bandwidth limits of the CMUT instead. This is plotted in Figure 3.11. The upper and
lower limits are shown as function of cell radius for three diﬀerent resonance frequencies. The
choice of resonance frequencies plotted here will be explained in Section 3.4. The total bandwidth
is decreasing as the boundaries come closer for larger radii. The lower limit increases linearly,
whereas the upper limit seems to decrease logarithmic. Looking at these plots can give a better
overview, if the designer of the device already knows at which frequencies, measurements would
be done. Larger bandwidth can result in a more information rich signal, but as that means lower
pressure output, it can also result in the loss of information. Therefore these plots served to aid
in the ﬁnal decision on the parameters for the design of the CMUT cells.
When an approximate choice has fallen in terms of bandwidth, information and pressure output,
it is time to look at the allowed cell radii for a given pitch. As described in Section 2.2 the pitch
is an important parameter in the ﬁnished probe as it determines the amount of side-lobes in the
signal, which will interfere with the main signal. If the equations described in that section are
employed the result is several bands of pitches allowed for a given number of cells in a row in an
element. This is plotted in Figure 3.12. A cutoﬀminimum bandwidth of 90 percent was chosen so
that cell radius with lower bandwidths are not plotted. The cell radii shown here is the radius plus
the cell-to-cell distance, so that a cell with radius 22.5 µm will be shown as 25 µm in this plot.
There has to be a small gap between cells, to allow for proper bonding in-between the cells. The
actual size of the cell determines the performance characteristics of the CMUT whereas this plot
contains what is actually possible in terms of fabrication. To produce these plots, the array was
chosen to have a height of 8 mm which is within the standard opening of a laparoscopic incision
[3]. The length of the array depends on the element width and pitch, which again depend on the
frequency and speed of sound in the medium. A speed of sound of 1480 m/s was used, which is
the speed of sound in water. Hexagonal packing was used in the calculation, as that would give
a constant ﬁll factor. In the ﬁgure can be seen that the bands overlap, meaning that for a given
pitch, several cell sizes can be used. These overlaps increases as the cutoﬀbandwidth is decreased.

3.2
Receive simulation
Similar plots can be made for the CMUT transducer in receive mode. Instead of outputting a signal
and measuring, the CMUT response to an incoming wave will be simulated here. Pressure output
will thus be replaced by pressure sensitivity given in units of V/MPa. The resonance frequency,
bandwidth and the pressure sensitivity are displayed in Figure 3.15 and 3.16. There is a similar
story when compared to the transmit plots though there are some changes. The surface plot for
bandwidth and information has been omitted since the conclusion are very similar to the transmit
case, or is a consequence of the pressure sensitivity. The plots have been constructed the same
way, where the contour lines for the resonance frequency have been plotted on top of the surface
plots.
The Resonance frequency is similar in that it increases with increasing plate thickness and
decreases with increasing radius. There is now a local maximum at a radius of around 30 µm and
plate thickness at around 8 µm. This was no maximum in the transmit mode. There also seems
to be maxima at low plate thicknesses and large radii, but this might be due to insuﬃcient data
points and numerical noise.
For the pressure sensitivity, there seems to be an optimal region for higher plate thickness, or a

3.3
Combined transmit and receive results, ’Pulse-echo’
While still in the frequency domain, the transmit and receive frequency spectra can be multiplied.
This is a rough estimate of the Pulse-Echo response of the CMUT. Then the standard -6dB limit of
the bandwidth can also be used instead of the approximate -3dB of either the transmit or receive
response. Bandwidth and information surface plots have been omitted again due to lack of new
information. Going through the same plots as in the previous sections gives similar results;
The resonance frequency is almost the same in Pulse-Echo mode in Figure 3.18 as for Transmit
mode, but quite diﬀerent from the Receive mode. The resonance frequency decreases with increas-
ing radius and increases with increasing plate thickness which holds for the Pulse-Echo mode as
well.
In transmit mode a voltage is applied to the CMUT and a pressure wave is emitted giving a
unit of MPa per Voltage Out. In receive mode a pressure wave is received and a voltage signal is
measured giving a unit of Voltage In per MPa. As the signals have been multiplied, the unit must
therefore be Voltage In per Voltage Out which is a measure of the ’eﬃciency’ of the CMUT. In
Figure 3.19 This has been plotted. The same relation as for the previous modes holds, though no
local maximum region in sensitivity/eﬃciency as in the receive mode can be seen. For these values
of R and h a maximum of around 70 % V/V can be obtained.
Finally looking at the low and high limits of the bandwidth, they seem to follow the same
trends as in the transmit and receive mode. For 10 MHz the low limit has a bit more logarithmic
slope compared to the receive and transmit mode.

3.4
Comparison of simulations to TABLA probe measure-
ments
Before ﬁnalizing on some parameters that could be interesting to test, the simulation was ﬁrst
tested against measurements done on the Tabla probes, which are linear arrays of circular CMUT
cells. An overview is seen in Table 3.1. The measurement results have been read oﬀof the plots
shown in Figure 3.21
Starting by comparing the resonance frequency for both modes and for diﬀerenet probes. It can
be seen that for both modes, tabla V and VI have measured resonance frequencies lying close to
the simulated ones. Table HF however, lies very far away from the simulated value. Characteristic
for this probe, is that it has a smaller cell radius than the other two probes, which might show that
that the simulation does not capture small cell radius behavior very well. Another characteristic
is that it has a higher resonance frequency than the other two, which in turn is more susceptible
to loss. Or it might be that the system has a high absorption rate at higher frequencies [20],[1]
p. 74. Here the system is seen as all of the electrical components and all extra materials with
which the sound wave interacts such as the lens of the probe. Furthermore it could be due to
substrate ringing. Substrate ringing is destructive interference cause by sound reﬂections in the
substrate generated by the CMUT. The same behaviour is seen in the low bandwidth limit, where
the simulation actually predicts a worse value than what is actually measured for the pulse-echo
mode. For the High bandwidth limit, the simulation does not perform well at higher frequencies
again. It performs best for Tabla VI which has the lowest, high bandwidth limit out of the three.
For the bandwidth, calculated by Equation 2.2, Tabla VI and HF both perform better than
==PAGE:40==
3.5. DECIDING ON CELL RADII AND PLATE THICKNESS FOR MASK DESIGN
35
Figure 3.15: Resonance frequency plotted versus radius of CMUT cells and plate thickness in
receive mode of operation. The points at which the TABLA probes should lie according to the
simulation, have also been plotted for reference. Contour lines for the resonance frequency have
also been plotted.
predicted. In the transmit mode, the bandwidth is very diﬀerent between measured and simulated
results. For Tabla V it diﬀers the most, probably due to the large deviation in the high bandwidth
limit. For Tabla HF the increase in bandwidth might be especially due to large decrease in the
low bandwidth limit.
For the signal eﬃciency in Pulse-Echo mode, the discrepancies are very large. Especially for
Tabla HF. Tabla VI even performs better than the simulation.
Again, it might be frequency
dependent. Neglecting Tabla HF, it seems that Tabla V and VI both follow the same trend as
seen in the simulation, where plate thickness increases signal eﬃciency but reduces bandwidth. As
such, the simulation is used as a guide to approximate device speciﬁcations. Kept in mind should
be that the high bandwidth limits are approximately halved when measuring.
3.5
Deciding on Cell radii and plate thickness for mask de-
sign
Two main criteria was used in choosing the parameters for the mask design.
The ﬁrst was a
performance characteristic; The desired Center Frequency for a Laparascopy probe and the desired
high and low bandwidth limits as well as the bandwidth itself. The second was design study related;
Several interesting things happens in the low cell radius/ High resonance frequency regime going
from simulation to measurements. This was also seen as a project serving to aid the further work
==PAGE:41==
36
CHAPTER 3. SIMULATION OF DESIGN PARAMETERS
Figure 3.16: Pressure Sensitivity plotted versus radius of CMUT cells and plate thickness in receive
mode of operation. The points at which the TABLA probes should lie according to the simulation,
have also been plotted for reference. Contour lines for the resonance frequency have also been
plotted.
of PhD student Stine Løvholt Grue Pedersen so there should be a certain spread in the parameters,
so as to cover more of the design space. Generally the design was made as a high-risk high-reward,
but with safety stops so that some successes could be achieved even if the project did not proceed
perfectly as planned.
Figure 3.1 was the starting point. Looking at this plot, it can be seen that for lower values of
plate thickness and cell radius, the contour lines are closer. This means that smaller variations
should cause a visible change in performance. It is also at this end of the parameter spectrum
there is the largest discrepancy between simulation and measurements. As high bandwidth was
a requirement, it was also the region for lower values that was of interest as seen in Figure 3.3.
Setting a cutoﬀminimum bandwidth at 90 % Figure 3.12 was consulted in order to see which
values of cell radius was allowed and what pitch they could correspond to in the bands. As the
optimal pitch is λ
2 some cell radii at around this value should be chosen. A value of around 0.7 λ
was also chosen. This would allow for more bands to be used, increasing the design space. It also
accommodate a value, which was a little less than the optimal value was. Because of unknowns,
such as bonding problems, the optimal λ
2 might not work, or there might be too low signal coming
from them. As phantom imaging was not planned during this project, it could also be argued
that only the optimal or sub-optimal pitch should be used, and other parameters studied instead.
However from the above stated reasons, two pitches were chosen. This would allow for a cell radius
approximately between 19 and 33 µm.
Then Figure 3.11 was consulted in order to ﬁnd a cell radius at 7.5 MHz. In order to have

photo-lithography this amounts to an error that can be around 10 % of the lowest cell radii if the

3.6
Summary
In this chapter the simulations made by Postdoc Mathias Engholm was analyzed. There was found
to be a bandwidth-sensitivity trade-oﬀ, meaning that high bandwidth and high sensitivity could
not be attained simultaneously for CMUT devices. The bandwidth was found to increase with
increasing cell radius and decreasing plate thickness. The opposite was the case for sensitivity.
It was found that for a constant resonance frequency, the bandwidth decreases and sensitivity
increases with increasing cell radius due to an underlying dependence on plate thickness. The
results of the simulations were compared to measurements on the TABLA probes where it showed
that they performed worse in terms of bandwidth and sensitivity, probably due to losses in the
complete system. A general rule of thumb was to double/halve the low and high bandwidth limits
from simulation to actual probes.
Call radii 19.5, 22.5 and 31 µm was chosen with expected
resonance frequencies 10, 7.5 and 4 MHz.
==PAGE:46==
3.6. SUMMARY
41
Figure 3.22: Resonance frequency in a color plot as a function cell radius and plate thickness
for Pulse-Echo Mode. Transparent vertical black lines display the choice of cell radii, with the
thickness of the line representing uncertainty. Transparent horizontal red line display the choice of
plate thickness, with the thickness of the line representing uncertainty.

Chapter 4
Design of mask
To better understand the mask design, an overview of the process ﬂow is initially presented in
this chapter. Afterwards an explanation of the convention of both the mask layer and also array
placement is given. Then the mask design is presented with a discussion of design choices, as well
as an explanation of how certain design were incorporated in the mask design software L-edit. This
is split into a section for the arrays, and a section for the alignment marks.
4.1
Process ﬂow overview
To get a better overview of the diﬀerent mask designed in this project, and also to get a better
overview of the processes, the process ﬂow will be presented here. The 1st LOCOS process steps
are presented in the following list corresponding to Figure 4.1.
(A) A layer of oxide is grown. Then nitride is deposited. Then polySilicon is deposited. Finally
(photo)resist is spin-coated. The resist will only be displayed in these ﬁrst three steps.
(B) By using the mask ’Bump’, the Si-bump pattern is deﬁned in the resist
(C) The polySilicon is etched transferring the pattern to the polySilicon layer.
(D) Resist is stripped.
(E) The pattern is transferred to the nitride layer by etching the nitride.
(F) PolySilicon is stripped
(G) 1st LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) process
(H) Nitride is stripped
(I) Oxide is stripped
(J) A layer of oxide is grown, then nitride is deposited and ﬁnally polySilicon is deposited for the
2nd LOCOS process.
The 2nd LOCOS processes for wafer batch 1 and 2 and subsequent bonding is displayed in the
following lists corresponding to Figure 4.2.
(K1) Using the ’Cavity’ mask a nitride oxidation barrier is deﬁned for the cavities. Using the
’DREM’ mask, an opening into the isolation oxide is made.
(L1) A trench is etch through the opening using the DREM etch.
(M1) 2nd LOCOS process is performed deﬁning post oxide and ﬁlling the trench. (wafer batch 1
was stopped at this step)
(N1) polySOI wafer is fusion bonded to the substrate wafer
42
==PAGE:48==
4.1. PROCESS FLOW OVERVIEW
43
Figure 4.1: Overview of the ﬁrst LOCOS process in a schematic. The steps are explained in the
list above the ﬁgure.
(O1) The polySilicon on the backside is removed, the ’Box’ oxides are removed and the handle
layer is removed.
(P1) Aluminum is deposited
(Q1) The wafer is etched/lapped(grinded) from the backside separating the elements. As shown
in O2) and P2) contact have also been made to the bottom, but is made inside every single
element if not strictly connected from the backside.
(K2) Using the ’Cavity’ mask a nitride oxidation barrier is deﬁned for the cavities.
(L2) 2nd LOCOS process is performed deﬁning post oxide and cavity
(M2) polySOI wafer is fusion bonded to the substrate wafer.
(N2) The polySilicon on the backside is removed, the ’Box’ oxides are removed and the handle
layer is removed.
(O2) Using the ’Etch to bottom’ mask, the top plate and post oxide is etched to electrically contact
the substrate
