4.2
Conventions for Lapmut design
Figure 4.3 shows the naming convention of the arrays. L, M and R stands for Left, Middle and
Right respectively. The numbering goes from bottom to top. The ﬁgure also shows that the M
arrays are wider than L and R. Throughout most of Chapter 6 and 7 array M5 is used as a test array
to perform measurements on. In Chapter 8, Array M5, L4 and M2 are electrically characterized.
Figure 4.4 shows the 5 designed photolithography masks in a part of the M5 array. The grey
inner circles represent the bump mask, and these mark a pattern that will not be etched. The
Yellow outer circles represent the cavity mask, and these mark a pattern that will not be etched.
The brown lines separating the elements, are the DREM mask. The brown lines will be etched.
The red mask is the Aluminum etch mask and the red region will not be etched/removed. The
purple color denote the etch to bottom mask and these regions will be etched. Note that the
aluminum mask is hidden underneath the etch bottom mask on these pads.
4.3
Design of linear arrays
On the basis of Chapter 3 the design parameters of the CMUT arrays were chosen and the design
was made in the L-edit software version 15.22. The overview of chosen design parameters can be
seen in the wafer map in Figure 4.5. The wafer map shows a total of 18 diﬀerent array designs.
14 out of 18 arrays have 192 elements, which is the same as for the TABLA probes which are
also linear arrays. It could be argued that instead of having fewer large arrays, there could be
several more test arrays. These test arrays would probably also be safer in fx that there is a larger
bonding area between elements. However, as will also be seen later in Chapter 6, some problems
do not arise before certain thresholds. Thus, when doing process optimisation, it might seem as if
the process works, which it in fact might not, when going to a more complete/ﬁnal structure. It is
a delicate balance when doing semi-isolated experiments in a project, that is going to sum up to
a ﬁnal product. And to be able to arrive at a ﬁnished product, meanwhile making sure that the
problems solved during the project, will actually be useful in the ﬁnal product. The linear arrays
in the double LOCOS had already been fabricated successfully in the group before. Incorporating
the DREM etch and trench reﬁll, would probably also deliver certain unknown problems. Thus it
was decided that full 192 linear arrays at the desired pitch would be fabricated so as to encounter
the problems when actually having to make the structures. Meanwhile if the process worked, or
it would come to work, the amount of data that could be extracted would be large. And so, the
information that could be gained either way would be very valuable to PhD student Stine Løvholt
Grue Pedersen as part of her PhD project, while still being able to reach some conclusions in this
thesis.
As described in chapter 3 three radii were chosen to be tested, 22.5 µm, 19.5 µm 31 µm, which
have been given Green, Blue and Yellow colors respectively in Figure 4.5. Also described in the
chapter, was that two pitches were chosen; Around 0.5 and 0.7 λ pitch, where the center arrays
have the latter pitch. This is also evident in Figure 4.3 where it can be seen that the center arrays
are wider than the arrays to the left and right side. Finally, as the DREM etch, depending on
the reﬁll method, takes up a signiﬁcant portion of the available bonding area, several distances
between cell and trench edge were chosen. The distances were 2.5 µm, 5 µm and 11 µm. The 2.5
µm was seen as a very risky design whereas the 11 µm was suspected to be a safe design based on
the results of simulation in [15]. An example of the structures can be seen for the center array M5
in Figure 4.4.
As a rule of thumb the bump radius should be 2
3 of the cavity/cell radius of the CMUT cells.
This is due to the Bird’s beak structure of the double LOCOS process. If the bump radius is too
large compared to the cavity radius, the slope of the bump will move into the slope of the cavity,
causing problems in the oxidation and bonding. Likewise if it is to small, then the ﬁeld that pulls
down on the top plate will be very conﬁned, and thus there will be diﬀerent plate bending shapes
==PAGE:50==
4.3. DESIGN OF LINEAR ARRAYS
45
Figure 4.2: Overview schematic of the two diﬀerent 2nd LOCOS processes and bonding afterwards
used in this project. 1’s corresponds to the fabrication employed for the 1st batch of wafers in this
project where integration of DREM was tested. 2’s corresponds to the seconds batch of wafers
used in this project where top plate separation was used. The steps are explained in the lists above
the ﬁgure.
==PAGE:51==
46
CHAPTER 4. DESIGN OF MASK
Figure 4.3: How the arrays ﬁll out the wafer. The naming convention is also shown where L, M,
R stands for Left, Middle, Right and the numbering is from bottom to top.
and pull-in. Process simulations done later in the project would show that this ratio would be ﬁne
for the given LOCOS processes. This is shown in Chapter 5.
For each cell radius, the pitch between the cells were set to be 2R+5 µm. There has to be a
ﬂat region in-between cells in order for the plate to bond properly and conﬁne each cell to itself.
This distance was primarily based on the fact that this was the parameter set in the simulation
from the previous chapter. It was earlier shown that this would give a suﬃcient ﬂat area between
cells [22]. However the thickness of the post oxide was not as large, and the post oxide slope length
(See Chapter 1) increases with increasing bump or cavity height [21]. Thus it might turn out that
the ﬂat region between the Si-bump slope (See Chapter 1) and the post oxide slope might not be
suﬃcient. A compromise was struck due to element separation and distance between cells and the
DREM trench, which is discussed in the following.
In a hexagonal pattern this corresponds to the cells being shifted up by 2R + 5 µm if there
were no elements. As there is a certain element separation, there has to be an additional upwards
shift:
==PAGE:52==
4.3. DESIGN OF LINEAR ARRAYS
47
Figure 4.4: Figure showing an example of the design in array M5
kshift =
p
(2((R + a) + dt) + dt)2 −(2(R + a) + dt)2 −
√
3((R + a) + dt)
(4.1)
Where dt is cell to DREM trench distance, a is half the inter-cell distance and R is the cavity
radius. This ensures that the cells will have the same minimum diagonal distance to the trench
width. It will also cause an extra inter cell distance. This can be seen in Figure 4.6.
This resulted in a compromise. It was deemed very important to have a certain distance to the
DREM trench. The trench is very susceptible to oxidation in a bonding point of view. Thus it was
necessary to keep this distance constant in order be able to asses when the trench-to-cell distance
was insuﬃcient. Another way to work around this issue would be to move the cells away from
each other horizontally, but then the pitch would increase even further. In the end, the horizontal
inter-cell distance was kept at 5 µm, while the diagonal inter cell distance was dependent on trench-
to-cell distance. Thus as high a ﬁll factor as possible could be achieved while retaining a suﬃcient
bonding area between elements. This also demonstrates the balance between fabrication/yield
optimisation and performance criteria of the CMUT. Increasing the element separation decreases
both ﬁll-factor and pitch, while increasing bonding area and possibly yield.
The DREM trenches were drawn using the wire tool in L-edit and setting the width of the wire
to 2.5 µm. The corners of the full wire array was subsequently rounded using the ﬁllet tool, with
an inner(concave) radius of 0.5 µm and outer(convex) radius of 2.5µm. The DREM etch is aspect
ratio dependent as described in Chapter 2. This causes diﬀerences in the width of the trench
opening to etch in varying depths. This is not desired, and so the corners were rounded to make
the opening as similar as possible.
The height of the elements was chosen to be 6 mm, as there also needed to be suﬃcient space
for contact pads and probing. The needles used for electrical impedance characterization has a
ﬁxed separation of 200 µm, thus to contact top plate and the substrate at least 400 µm are needed
on the top and bottom of the array. In addition, these pads should be well isolated electrically.
The contact pads for individual elements were placed on every second element on the top and
bottom of the array. Then the width of the pad can be larger than the width of the element. An
example can be seen in Figure 4.7. Not visible is that there is also going to be aluminum on top
of the etch to bottom area. The white space around the bottom contact will be where aluminum
is etched making sure that there is an electrical separation.
Additionally a design in which the top plate was separated was also made, to test the diﬀerence
between substrate separated elements and top plate separated elements on the same wafer. This is
shown in Figure 4.8. A single array was designed in which only back contacting would be possible,
to see if it was feasible incorporating it into the same process ﬂow as the rest of the arrays as
shown in Figure 4.9.
==PAGE:53==
48
CHAPTER 4. DESIGN OF MASK
Figure 4.5: Wafer map of design parameters.
Some Test arrays were also speciﬁcally designed for acoustic measurements. These structures
were also designed to be safer with a large element separation and distance between cells and
DREM trench. An example can be seen in Figure 4.10. The DREM gap test arrays was designed
with various dt to see if there were some threshold in bonding.
==PAGE:54==
4.4. DESIGN OF ALIGNMENT MARKS
49

4.4
Design of alignment marks
Figure 4.11 shows an overview of the alignment marks used in the Lapmut design.
For the alignment structures, inspiration was sought in the previous work of the group and
also on the internet. 4 diﬀerent alignment structures were incorporated to ensure quick and easy
alignment. Firstly the arrows pointing to the alignment marks was designed as in the design in
[23]. Instead of arrows or triangles, pie wedges were used [23] p. 59. This gives easy and intuitive
control in placing them on top of each other at diﬀerent sizes. An example of these in the Lapmut
design are shown in Figure 4.12a.
Secondly Magnus Galsg˚ard Petersen and Andreas Havreland found that instead of aligning
crosses and squares to each other, smaller geometries centered in larger geometries works better
[24]. This is because the human eye is better at seeing relative diﬀerences rather than absolute
==PAGE:55==
50
CHAPTER 4. DESIGN OF MASK
Figure 4.8: Example of the plate separation array structure.
Figure 4.9: Example of the exclusively backside contact array structure.
diﬀerences. It can then be easier to see whether a circle for example, is centered within another
circle. This was incorporated in the Lapmut design as shown in Figure 4.12b where circle within
circles were used.
Finally the use of Vernier structures such as the ones used on calipers have also been employed.
A Vernier scale also uses relative distances to see absolute variations. By having two sets of lines,
each set with a diﬀerent pitch, one can gauge the precise misalignment. For perfect alignment the
center lines will be straight across one another. In misalignment, two oﬀ-center lines will be across
one another. As the diﬀerence in pitch is known, by counting the number of lines from the center
to the oﬀ-center lines, one has a resolution in misalignment on the order of the pitch. This is also
shown in Figure 4.12b.
The aspect ratio dependent etching when using the DREM needed to be accommodated in
==PAGE:56==
4.5. SUMMARY
51
Figure 4.10: Example of test array for acoustic measurement
the alignment marks as well.
No openings should have a width much larger than the width
of the trenches etched in the actual arrays.
This would cause unnecessary holes in the wafer
and potentially damage the chuck on which the wafer sits in the dry etching equipment. This
is accommodated by ﬁrst making the alignment structures as intended and afterwards using a
boolean operation in the software. Thus a the actual design can be split into a set of lines as seen
on the pie wedge arrow in Figure 4.12a and the alignment mark for aligning the DREM etch to
the cavity layer in Figure 4.13a
Another issue in the alignment marks arose from the heights introduced during the LOCOS
process. Some of the alignment marks would result in a bump being oxidised instead being covered
by nitride. This would result in very tall structures which could be a problem in bonding. A ﬁx
was incorporated by applying a DREM etch to the bump structure so that there would be an
oxidation on the side walls. That could inhibit the growth in the oxidation in the as the Silicon
could be consumed by the side wall oxidation. The ﬁx is shown in Figure 4.13b.
4.5
Summary
In this chapter an overview of the process ﬂow was shown. The convention used for naming the
arrays and masks was presented. The motivation behind the design of the arrays were given, where
a balance was struck between a risky and a more safe design, leaning towards a riskier one. The
diﬀerent types of arrays were presented, and new alignment marks including the work of previous
design as well as a new Vernier scale were shown.

Chapter 5
Process simulation using Athena
Silvaco
In this chapter, simulations using the process simulator Athena by Silvaco will be used to esti-
mate the viability of the process. Process optimisation of the double LOCOS process was done in
ATHENA by Silvaco, simultaneously with fabrication in the cleanroom, as the software was ﬁrst
available some weeks into the project (after the design phase). Several ideas of optimising the
process was tested using the software.
Figure 5.1: Figure from athena showing the structure just before the simulation crashes when the
2 oxide fronts meets. The grid is shown as green lines. This ﬁgure also shows that the nitride mask
is below the post oxide, even before the full oxidation time.
54
==PAGE:60==
55
It will also be used to gain insights as to how to improve the process. A major issue when
separating the elements, by etching the substrate, is that the bonding area decreases as shown
in [22]. When separating elements etching the top plate, it also results in lower bond area, but
these separations can be well deﬁned. When separating through the substrate, the etched trenches
can also be well deﬁned. But it is the further processing that severely decreases the bond area,
for example by oxidation. This especially true if the trench is to be completely reﬁlled by oxide.
This is sketched in Figure 5.2, where A) corresponds to top plate separation of elements, B) to a
DREM etch with no ﬁlling and C) to a DREM etch that has been ﬁlled by oxidation. The dashed
line indicates the non-bonded section for the top plate separation case. From the dashed line it is
clearly visible how there is less bonding interface between the polySilicon top plate and the post
oxide in case C).
One of the project aims was to try and integrate trench ﬁlling of DREM trenches with the
second LOCOS step. Therefore, Athena could be used to test how the design would perform, and
how the process parameters could be altered to get the best results in the future. There are other
possibilities for incorporation of DREM etching and trench ﬁlling. As described in Chapter 4 the
alignment marks were made so that the DREM etch and reﬁll could be incorporated at other steps
in the process in the future. The feasibility of this could be tested using the process simulations
software Athena.
First some preliminary convergence tests will be made using Athena.
As it would require
several days for one simulation of a 100 µm deep trench, the trench should be more shallow in the
simulations. It was ﬁrst tested how deep it should be, to be able to assume the 100 µm depth.
Then a convergence test was made on the grid of the oxide grown. Several other aspects were
tested, but are not directly displayed in this report. This includes the eﬀect of using a thicker
nitride oxidation barrier. The nitride layer is the cause of the birds beak structure seen when
doing a local oxidation of silicon. This is can be seen in Figure 5.1 at x= 20 µm. As the nitride
Figure 5.2: Schematic of the diﬀerent scenarios of bonding interface. A) corresponds to top plate
separation of elements, B) to a DREM etch with no ﬁlling and C) to a DREM etch that has been
ﬁlled by oxidation. The dashed line indicates the non-bonded section for the top plate separation
case. From the dashed line it is clearly visible how there is signiﬁcantly less bonding interface
between the polySilicon top plate and the post oxide in case C).

is already stiﬀ, making it thicker can cause a variation in the proﬁle of the bird’s beak structure.
This might inﬂuence the amount of bondable area in the elements. In [22], a lot of testing on the
LOCOS process was simulated. In the simulations, certain parameters for nitride viscosity were
used. This resulted in proﬁles, which were very similar to actual processed proﬁles measured using
AFM and SEM. However, these parameters were diﬀerent from the parameters already included
in Athena, which has also been ﬁtted to proﬁles measured using SEM [25]. Changing the nitride
viscosity can have a huge impact on the oxide/LOCOS proﬁle. Validation of these parameters were
viewed as out of scope for this project, and thus further experiments remains to be made. In the
forthcoming simulations, the standard parameters included in Athena were used.
Another test in which only a few insights was gained was doing a convergence test on the mesh
grid for the substrate rather than the grown oxide. As with other Finite Element software, deﬁning
the correct grid is crucial to getting realistic results. Even more so in Athena. A general rule of
thumb is to make the grid denser in critical regions such as on interfaces and corners. Furthermore,
because an oxidation is also consuming silicon, this means that the mesh will be removed and re-
meshed while simulating. This is another less trivial task for the simulation software. More so, it
is also quite important that the mesh matches well across boundaries. If there is a problem with
any of these points, the simulation will crash. This is not always an issue, but when reﬁlling the
trench with an oxidation, where two oxidation fronts collide, the simulations done in this project
crashed rather often. In Figure 5.1.
There were found no golden way to ﬁx this issue, such as just making the mesh ﬁner. It seemed
as if it was more of a question on mesh matching rather than roughness. Therefore, if a substrate
with a grid was found to work, that grid was used. Further optimisation could deﬁnitely be done
however, and the results should be interpreted as general tendencies rather than absolute values.
Values from the simulations, will however be used to gain insight into these general tendencies.

5.1
Trench depth convergence test
Throughout the convergence test a diﬀerence between proﬁles is calculated.
This diﬀerence is
deﬁned as;
Dif =
Pi | (yn,i −yn−1,i) |
xn −xn−1
(5.1)
where y is the point of the top most boundary of the oxide, with the the subscript n denoting
the current proﬁle, and the subscript i denoting the point on the top most boundary. x denotes
the parameter varied, and thus the denominator is the parameter step. It is simply the diﬀerence
between the proﬁles normalised to the parameter step length. The reason why a relative deviation
was not chosen, is due to the fact, that the actual proﬁle made during processing is not known.
Using a relative deviation would give the impression that this was known.
First the trench depth was studied to see what trench depth was necessary before the diﬀerence
in the oxide proﬁle did not change anymore. The trench was varied from a depth of 30 to 5 µm.
The top oxide proﬁle is shown in Figure 5.3. The middle of the trench can be seen by the sudden
dip in the proﬁle at around 29 µm. The slope length of the proﬁle is then deﬁned as the distance
from the middle of the trench until the slope is approximately ﬂat. Here it can be seen, that as
the trench becomes deeper, the separation in the top of the oxide becomes wider above the trench.
The slope length on either side of the middle of the trench also becomes longer.
The diﬀerence as a function of the trench depth is shown in Figure 5.4. From the ﬁgure can be
seen, that the error decreases as the trench becomes deeper. At a depth of 15 µm the error reach
a stagnating level where not much is gained by making the trench deeper. The trench was thus
set to 15 µm for future simulations.
The simulated structured from a shallow and a deep trench is compared in Figure 5.5. Here can
be seen that for the shallow trench, the bottom corners of the trench aﬀects the top part of the

5.2
Convergence test on oxide grid during growth
A study on how the grid of the oxide grown aﬀected the oxide proﬁle was also done as a convergence
test. The way the process simulator Athena grows an oxide is described in the Athena User Manual
[17]. Here will be given a general outline on top of what is described in Section 2.6. There are
two main methods used in this project, one is what is called the Compress model and the other is
called Viscous.
The oxidation model uses the Deal and Grove model described earlier. It calculates the ﬂux
of oxygen at every grid point in the oxide ﬁlm. This includes the boundaries of oxide/gas and
oxide/silicon. The ﬂux at any other boundary is set to 0, meaning that that the Athena model
does not account for oxidation of the nitride layer, or how it functions as an oxidation barrier. By
ﬁnding the ﬂux and thus the concentration, the velocity of the oxide molecules at the Oxide/Silicon
interface can be calculated. By multiplying the velocity by the time step the program takes, it
can calculate the thickness of the oxide grown in that time step. This also assumes the process is
diﬀusion limited. The thickness of the oxide layer determines how much of the Silicon is consumed
each time step and is 44 % of the oxide thickness. Thus by setting the grid of the oxide grown, it
is essentially telling the program how large time steps it should use. The standard grid setting is
growing the oxide 0.1 µm every time step.
The compress model is the standard model in Athena, and does not take stresses in the ﬁlm
into account. It calculates the oxide viscosity by Young’s modulus and Poisson’s ratio to ﬁnd the
ﬂow of the oxide elements.
The viscous model builds on top of that and takes stresses into account.
It calculates the
==PAGE:66==
5.2. CONVERGENCE TEST ON OXIDE GRID DURING GROWTH
61
15
20
25
30
35
x [µm]
19.4
19.6
19.8
20
20.2
y [µm]
0.1 µm oxide grid
0.075 µm oxide grid
0.05 µm oxide grid
0.03 µm oxide grid
0.02 µm oxide grid
Figure 5.8: Figure showing the proﬁle of the oxide grown for 500 minutes at various mesh densities
using the compress model. The diﬀerence between proﬁles seem to get larger with smaller grid
size.
stresses from a set of pre-exponential and activation energy terms, where the stresses are thus
dependent on the temperature. The diﬀusion constant, viscosity and reaction rate constant are
all made stress-dependent and they are calculated after the stresses have been solved for. This
process continues iteratively until it converges. Thus for every time-step another set of iterative
calculations are done, which heavily increases the simulation time.
In the following study, all oxidations were done in a wet atmosphere at 1100 ◦C and at 1
atm pressure. Several grid sizes were studied at diﬀerent oxidation times. The program began
crashing at a grid size of around 0.02 µm, which was gauged to be due to insuﬃcient computing
power(probably RAM) of the pc it was run on. Thus it eﬀectively set the limit of how small the
grids could be. Though Athena can output the structure at every time step, it does not allow the
user to set these time steps. Therefore, to compare the grid sizes the simulation was run from the 0
minutes to the desired time, and restarted at 0 minutes for the next desired oxidation time. Also,
Athena does an unknown post-oxidation step not known to the author at the time of writing which
alters the structure as seen in Figure 5.6. Here can be seen that the oxidation after 1516 minutes
still has a void, whereas the 1500 minutes oxidation has no voids. It is expected that oxidising
for longer times will ﬁll voids, albeit very slowly. The opposite has happened here. It could be
that Athena does an annealing step afterwards, or smooths the structure, so that the computation
becomes easier. Thus the oxidation has to be restarted if a speciﬁc time is desired to compare
diﬀerent structures/parameters.
Figure 5.7 shows the oxide proﬁle after an oxidation has been done at 2000 minutes for various
oxide grid sizes. From the proﬁles can be seen that there is a tendency for the proﬁle to shift down
for decreasing grid sizes. To the right in the ﬁgure can be seen that this down-shift also causes the
trench to get wider.
A similar trend is seen in Figure 5.8. At 1100 ◦C and for a trench width of 2.5 µm, the oxide
fronts merge at around 900 minutes oxidation time. Thus at 500 minutes, the oxidation fronts
have not yet merged. At this time the oxide thickness is also substantially thinner, and so more

of the initial proﬁle has remained. There is a small degree of lifting at the trench edges, especially

Looking at Figure 5.10 can be seen the diﬀerence between proﬁle as a function of the grid size.

that the slope increases for higher oxidation times. The grid size enhances this diﬀerence. The
slope can be seen to increase with decreasing grid sizes. This means that the result of the proﬁle
actually diverges when looking at just this plot. This might also be due to being to far from
convergence. At the very least, it seems as if rougher grids gives better results. Looking at Figure
5.9, there are more irregularities for the ﬁner mesh, suggesting that the solution is not converging
for ﬁner meshes. This might be due to the discontinuity at the corners, or mesh mismatch between
the oxide and silicon.

pattern of a down shift for ﬁner meshes can be seen. The diﬀerence between the proﬁles are also

the slope of the proﬁle is decreasing ﬁrst, then comes to a small local maximum, and thereafter
decreases.
The proﬁle after 500 minutes in Figure 5.12 also has visibly smaller diﬀerences, even compared
to after 1500 minutes. When going in from the right side the proﬁle seem to decrease. This could
mean, that there is some lifting induced when the oxide fronts meets.
In Figure 5.13 is seen the ’slope’ of the diﬀerence in proﬁles versus the mesh grid size. Though
there are not a lot of data points, the values seem to increase a little and then decrease for the ﬁnest
mesh, if not just decreasing. This could indicate that the mesh is beginning to converge. If that is
true, then there is a small bit of downshift and widening of the proﬁles for ﬁner meshes, meaning
that when using a rough mesh, one should keep in mind, that the result is on the optimistic side.

5.3
Study on distance from designed cavity to trench edge
With a small insight into the convergence, two diﬀerent studies were undertaken. The ﬁrst being
how the designed distance between cavity edge and trench edge would inﬂuence the bonding area.
The cavity edge is the same as the nitride edge before oxidation. A criteria for a successful bond
can be estimated in several ways. The group has previously taken the slope of the proﬁle [22],
[21], and looked at when the slope was below a certain percent. Alternatively, a criteria based on
an elastomechanical consideration, can give an estimate for bonding [26], [27]. This estimate was
found to be;
hlocal = 3.6
r γ
aE
(5.2)
Where hlocal is the height of a local height variation such as a spike or the height diﬀerence
between two point on a sloped surface. a is the radius extension of this variation, γ is the surface
energy and E is the reduced Young’s modulus
Y
1−ν2 . By ﬁxing the step length a, the maximum
height diﬀerence h for a successful bond can be found.
The surface energy of fusion bonding depends on a number of factors but has been found to be
very dependent on annealing [27] for Si-Si, Si-SiO2 and SiO2-SiO2 bonds. Annealing these bonds
generally give surface energies on the order of 1-3 J/m2 for anneal temperatures above 1000 ◦C
[28]. Room temperature bonding gives only a couple hundreds mJ/m2. In the following R has been
set to 20 nm and the viscous oxidation model was employed. This means that the bond length
over the proﬁle calculated is very local, and will not capture much larger non bonding structures.
In Figure 5.14 can be seen the proﬁle for diﬀerent distance between trench and nitride edge. For
a distance of 2.5 µm the proﬁle to the left can be seen to have a large height decrease, compared
to the proﬁle on the right. This is not desired as the bond will most likely not happen here. This
is an example of where the local bonding criteria is not optimal and R would have to be set higher

to capture larger scale bonding. Going to dt=5 µm lifts the proﬁle on the left almost up to the
proﬁle on the right so bonding could happen happen. This trend continues for longer distances. A
slope on the left side can be seen even for the largest distances, though it seems to level oﬀ.
The bond ratio was in this study deﬁned as the amount of bonding length divided by the length
from the designed cavity to the right side edge of the proﬁle. This is plotted for diﬀerent surface
energies in Figure 5.15. In the plot can be seen an almost linear increase for the lowest surface
energy. For the higher surface energies, it seems that quite a lot of bond rate is gained initially
which levels oﬀ. Higher surface energy also gives higher bond rate. These tendencies are to be
expected.
5.4
Study on the eﬀect of temperature during oxidation
The second study was on the eﬀect of temperature on the oxide proﬁle, and thus how it aﬀected
bonding. The proﬁles were oxidised to get a thickness of the post oxide of 2.85 µm. In Figure 5.16
is seen how the proﬁles vary with diﬀerent temperatures. A distance from the nitride edge to the
trench of 5 µm was used. From 1000 ◦C and below, there seem to be a longer ﬂat region on the
right side of the trench. The proﬁle on the left side of the trench also becomes more pointy and
taller. For higher temperature the proﬁles become increasingly ﬂatter on the left side, whereas
there is a larger non ﬂat right side.
In Figure 5.17 can be seen how this proﬁle change, and thus how the temperature aﬀects the
local bonding. The bond rate was normalised to a region extending from x=23 µm to the end of
the right side. The bond rate is again plotted for 3 diﬀerent surface energies and the bond rate
can be seen to increase with increasing energy as expected. The bond rate can also be seen to
increase with increasing temperature. This is probably due to the ﬂattening of the left side and the
increased but less sloped region on the right side. This is opposite with using the slope percentage
to ﬁnd a bonding point as was done in [21]. Overall they both describe how the proﬁle changes,
but whether to use one or the other deﬁnition remains to be tested.

5.5
Summary
In this chapter, the process of this project was studied using Athena Silvaco. A convergence test
was done on the oxide proﬁle for several cases. First the convergence as a function of the depth
of the trench was studied. It was found that a trench of 15 µm was suﬃcient. The density of
the grid of the oxide grown was also test for two a non-stress dependent (Compress) and a stress
dependent model (viscous) in Athena Silvaco. For the compress model it seemed as if convergence
was reached for rougher meshes. For the viscous model it seemed to converge for ﬁner meshes
though insuﬃcient computer power made it impossible to reach true convergence. The predicted
bond rate(bonding ratio) was also gauged for two diﬀerent studies. The bonding ratio increased
for increasing dt at a ﬁxed temperature. It increased with increasing temperature, for the bonding
criteria stated in this thesis. It also increased with increasing surface energy.
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 6
Fabrication - 1st Batch
Two runs of the process for the double LOCOS structure were made in this project. In the 1st
run, full process integration of the ﬁlled DREM etch was tried. The process ﬂow for this process
can be seen in Appendix C, where the process has been modiﬁed to accommodate the results of
this chapter. The main steps were illustrated in Figure 4.1 and 4.2 in Chapter 4. For clarity the
process for the 1st LOCOS is restated in the following list corresponding to Figure 6.1.
(A) A layer of oxide is grown. Then nitride is deposited. Then polySilicon is deposited. Finally
(photo)resist is spin-coated. The resist will only be displayed in these ﬁrst three steps.
(B) By using the mask ’Bump’, the Si-bump pattern is deﬁned in the resist
(C) The polySilicon is etched transferring the pattern to the polySilicon layer.
(D) Resist is stripped.
(E) The pattern is transferred to the nitride layer by etching the nitride.
(F) PolySilicon is stripped
(G) 1st LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) process
(H) Nitride is stripped
(I) Oxide is stripped
(J) A layer of oxide is grown, then nitride is deposited and ﬁnally polySilicon is deposited for the
2nd LOCOS process.
The process ﬂow was based on the process ﬂow of previous double LOCOS structures made in
the MEMS group. Throughout the chapter considerations and thoughts on the processing aspects
will be explained.
6.1
Choice of wafers
22 wafers that were n-type doped and had the lowest resistivity available from Nanolab were
bought. The intention was to contact the silicon substrate to electrically access the elements of the
transducer. Thus a low resistivity was required. This would reduce the resistance in the wafers.
6.2
Oxidation, nitride and polySilicon deposition
To be able to make a LOCOS structure, a Silicon nitride layer, or some oxidation barrier is needed,
to locally oxidise the Silicon. As there is tensile stress in grown or deposited nitride a thin oxide
(pad oxide) which has compressive stress is needed underneath to counteract that stress. It needs
to be relatively thin, so as to minimize the lateral inﬂux of oxidising species [22].
69
==PAGE:75==
70
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.1: Overview of the ﬁrst LOCOS process in a schematic. The steps are explained in the
list above the ﬁgure.
Before being able to oxidise the wafers, the wafers need to be cleaned thoroughly. This is done by
the RCA cleaning process. This cleaning process has been found to be useful in removing a number
of contaminants. The RCA cleaning procedure comprises of two primary steps with a number of
sub-steps in-between. The ﬁrst one, called RCA1, consists of a solution of NH4OH:H2O2:H2O
in the ratio 1:1:5. This solution will oxidise material, meaning it is great at removing organic
material. The Ammonium Hydroxide will also help in the solvation of metals such as Ni, Cu and
Au, or forming complexes with them. As the H2O2 will readily oxidise the surface and NH4OH
will slowly dissolve the oxide, particles can be dislodged. Wafers are dipped into this solution for
10 minutes at 70 ◦C and are afterwards rinsed in DI water for 3 minutes. The wafers can then
be dipped into an 5% HF solution for 30 seconds to completely remove any native or oxide formed
in this step. If they are subjected to the HF dip, they have to be DI-water rinsed for 3 minutes
before moving them to RCA2. RCA2 consists of HCl:H2O2:H2O in the ratio 1:1:5. This solution
removes heavy metals and metal hydroxides, and alkali metal ions that also might have been intro-
duced in the ﬁrst cleaning. Again they have to be rinsed for 3 minutes before an eventual HF dip.
The 22 wafers were subjected to the full cleaning procedure including the HF dips before oxidation.
After the cleaning the wafers can then be loaded into an oxidation furnace. This equipment
consists of a cylindrical quartz tube with surrounding resistive heaters, which are distributed so
that the temperature can remain uniform. This is crucial as the oxidation rate is very dependent
==PAGE:76==
6.2. OXIDATION, NITRIDE AND POLYSILICON DEPOSITION
71
on temperature. The wafers are loaded into the furnace in a quartz boat, where they stand upright
with a spacing of around half a centimetre. As described in Chapter 2, the oxidation can both
be done in a ’dry’ O2 atmosphere or a ’wet’ H2O atmosphere.
The 22 wafers were subjected
to a dry oxidation at 1050 ◦C for 90 minutes which should give a pad oxide thickness of 100
nm. The dry oxidation was used for better control of the oxide thickness at lower thicknesses. A
test wafer was placed in the middle of the quartz boat, for subsequent thickness measurements.
Several furnaces are capable of oxidation in the cleanroom, but only one was used throughout the
project. This one was called A3: Phosphorous Drive-In. To calculate the oxidation time, a matlab
script made by PhD student Martin Lind Ommen, was used. This script takes the most recent
cleanroom user logbook entries by users of the furnace (up to 50). These entries contains measured
oxide thicknesses for a given oxidation time, which are then used to compute the rate constants as
described in section 2.6.
To measure the layer thickness, an instrument called an Ellipsometer was used. This piece of
equipment sends polarised light into a surface at diﬀerent angles, and by measuring the change in
polarisation, optical constants and thicknesses of layers can be determined. The thickness of the
oxide layer was measured to be 104 nm. As purpose of the pad oxide is stress relief of the ntiride,
this deviation from 100 nm is ﬁne. After an RCA clean, the wafers are transported in a dedicated
clean wafer box. Due to the Nanolab cleanroom facility rules, the wafers can at most be stored for
24 hours in these boxes. After this period, they are deemed ’dirty’ again and therefore has to be
cleaned before going into the nitride deposition furnace. After the oxidation, the cleanroom had
to be closed for a week so the wafers could not be directly loaded into the subsequent furnace.
12 of the 22 wafers, along with a test wafer, were RCA cleaned again before being loaded into a
nitride deposition furnace. This furnace is a Low Pressure Chemical Vapor Deposition instrument
or LPCVD for short. In the furnace, a gas ﬂow of SiCl2H2, or DiChlorSilane and Ammonium , NH3,
is let into the furnace tube and react at the surface of the wafers. This results in stoichiometric
Si3N4 being deposited. The process is run at a low pressure of 150 mTorr, because diﬀusion from
the convective ﬂow down unto the wafer surface is then increased. This gives a faster deposition
rate, and the process can thus be run at a lower temperature, which would otherwise give a lower
deposition rate. As the temperature is lowered the deposition is in a reaction rate limited regime.
This means that the ﬂow of gases over the wafers will inﬂuence deposition less [16]. Then wafers
can be stacked as the deposition rate is reaction limited and not mass transfer limited. The wafers
were placed with a spacing of around 1 cm in the quartz boat. The only parameter that can be
changed for this furnace is the deposition time. This was set to 57 minutes as the furnace was
found to have a nitride deposition rate of 3.5nm/min, which would result in a 200 nm nitride
layer. In Figure 6.2 [29] can be seen the amount of nitride oxidised for several temperatures. This
corresponds to the nitride mask thickness needed.
To be sure that the nitride layer would suﬃce as an oxidation barrier of the silicon underneath,
the layer should be thick enough. From the plot, 200 nm was deemed to be more than suﬃcient.
Firstly, this can result in unsuccessful bonding, because the nitride layer edge will be lifted higher
than the bonding surface. But the Athena simulations showed that this should not happen (as
seen in Figure 5.1). Secondly, the following nitride etch and strip would take signiﬁcantly longer.
When designing the bump height and thickness of the post oxide and the width of the DREM
trench opening, the thickness of the nitride layer should be considered.
The thickness of the
deposited layer was then measured in the Ellipsometer to a thickness of 211 nm which was above
the desired 200 nm. As the nitride would be stripped later, this was no problem. After deposition
the outermost wafers had white particles on them and the nitride layer seemed nonuniform across
the wafer. This seemed to get better the further centered the wafers were placed in the quartz boat
The particles were deemed ’clean’ so the wafers did not have to be cleaned afterwards. This would
become a recurrent problem. Further testing of this was done in the second batch in Chapter 7
and will thus be explained there.
If the wafers are transferred in the dedicated clean boxes from one furnace to the next within 24
hours, they do not have to be cleaned beforehand. The wafers that had the most white particles,
were not processed further. These were the 2 outermost wafers out of the 12.
10 wafers were then transferred in a dedicated box into an LPCVD polySilicon furnace. This
furnace uses Silane, SiH4, for depositing polySilicon. The 10 wafers were placed in the quartz boat
with a spacing of around 0.5 cm. The function of the polySilicon layer is to be an etch mask for
==PAGE:77==
72
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.2: Figure showing the amount of nitride oxidised as a function of time for diﬀerent
temperatures. [29] This corresponds to the nitride mask thickness needed.
the nitride underneath. Thus the layer can be quite thin. The desired thickness was set to 100 nm.
As the deposition rate is dependent on the thickness of the layer, it can be hard to estimate the
time required to deposit a certain thickness. No exact 100 nm process had been run in the furnace
used, so the deposition time had to be gauged. This was done as in the case of the nitride furnace,
by consulting the online cleanroom user logbook. The deposition time was estimated to be 14
minutes giving a suspected deposition rate of 7.14 nm/min. The layer thickness was measured in
the FilmTek, which is a spectrophotometer, to be 110 nm. This was also ﬁne, as it was going to
be stripped later in the process. After the process, some of the white particles had been removed.
6.3
Lithography and deﬁnition of nitride mask by wet etch-
ing
After the layers have been deposited, photolithography can be done to deﬁne the bump structures
for the ﬁrst LOCOS process. The resist mask will be an etch barrier to the polySilicon.
The standard photolithography consists of a spin-coating of photoresist step, an exposure step
that deﬁne patterns in the photoresist and a development step that removes (un)exposed pho-
toresist. If the photoresist becomes insoluble in the developer when exposed to light with a given
wavelength it is called negative. If the opposite is true, it is called a positive resist.
The 10 wafers were spin-coated with the negative photoresist AZ nLOF 2020 with a thickness
of 1.5 µm. The spin-coating included HMDS priming which improves adhesion of the photoresist
to the underlying layer.
The wafers were then exposed in a S¨uss Microtec MA6 aligner, with
the ’Bump’ mask. They were exposed to 365 nm wavelength light at a constant intensity of 11
mW/cm2 for 10.2 seconds.
This was done in hard contact between the mask and the wafers,
meaning the mask and the wafers were pressed together during exposure. They were then post
exposure baked for 60 seconds at 110 ◦C, before being developed using a TMAH puddle on the
wafer for 60 seconds. They were visually inspected in the miscroscope to see if the small circles
had been deﬁned, which they had.
After the circles had been deﬁned in the photoresist, the polySilicon was wet etched. Wet
etching consists of submerging the wafers into a chemical bath/beaker.
The bath consists of
HNO3:BHF:H2O in the ratio 20:1:20, where the nitric acid oxidises Silicon, and BHF etches the
==PAGE:78==
6.3. LITHOGRAPHY AND DEFINITION OF NITRIDE MASK BY WET ETCHING
73
Figure 6.3: Image taken by an optical microscope after the 1st polySilicon etch, where the bump
mask hexagonal pattern of the bump circles are seen. the uniformity of the color indicates that
everything has been etched.
thin oxide formed. The bath loses potency over time (1+ months), but the mixture was made
just before the etch that the etch rate would not be too low. The etch rate is 50-200 nm/min
depending on the age of the solution - usually around 50-100 nm/min. The etch rate of oxide is
around 6 nm/min and for nitride it is close to 0 nm/min. A resist can withstand approximately
20-30 minutes in the bath. [30] The 10 wafers were spaced in every second place in the polymer
boat, which corresponds to around 1 cm and were submerged into the bath. They were etched
for 5 minutes, which should be a signiﬁcant overetch. An overetch means that the etch has been
continued even after the etching the desired thickness to account for a potentially lower etch rate.
This usually involves an etch stop, which can consist of another material at the bottom/sides of
the etched material. This material has a much lower etch rate in the given etch solution. For
example, Silicon nitride is an excellent etch stop to the polySilicon since the etch rate is very low
in nitride using polySilicon etch chemistry. After rinsing in DI-water and drying, the wafers were
inspected in an optical microscope. An image can be seen in Figure 6.3. It can be seen that there
is a uniform color diﬀerence which indicates that the etching has completed. For this array, M1,
the bump circles radius were measured to be 14 µm and they should have been 15 µm. This is less
than desired and it might inﬂuence the future pull-in and resonance frequency of the system, since
the force is applied over a lower area in the ﬁnal CMUT structure.
To be sure that the etch was successful, the next etching procedures were performed on one of
the wafers, now called W10. If the polySilicon had not been removed, the nitride etch would not
happen. This was done with the help of PhD student Rune Sixten Graas.
Before taking the wafer into a nitride etch bath, the remaining resist has to be stripped, this
can also be done wet, but was done dry throughout this project. A dry resist strip consists of
using O2 radicals generated by igniting a plasma consisting of O2 and N2 in a barrel etcher. A
metal perforated shield is placed around the wafers, which protects the wafers from the plasma.
The radicals then diﬀuse through the shield and reacts at the wafer surface. This was done for

W10 60 minutes at 1000 W power, 400 mL/min N2 ﬂow rate and 70 mL/min O2 ﬂow rate which
are standard process parameters. Note the diﬀerence in wording of removing layers; An etch is
a process which deﬁnes the structures, meaning some of the material being etched is left on the
wafer. A strip involves removing the rest of the material of a given layer.
The nitride etch bath consists of 85 % phosphoric acid, H3PO4, which should have an etch rate
of 2.7 nm/min in Si-rich Nitride and 4.5 nm/min in Stoichiometric Nitride [31]. Nanolab reports
2.6 nm/ min for Si-rich nitride. No testing in this thesis was done, however from earlier experience
in the MEMS group it was deemed to be around 2-2.5 nm/min. These values are for a temperature
at 160 ◦C, which the process is usually done at. After being etched for 90 minutes the wafer needs
to be cleaned as there might be alkali residuals left from the nitride bath. The nitride bath does
not introduce them itself, but because wafers from KOH baths are allowed to go directly into the
bath, some alkali metals might be left in there.
The wafer, W10, was then cleaned in a 7-up cleaning solution consisting of 98 % Sulfuric Acid,
H2SO4, in which Ammonium persulfate, (NH4)2S2O8 is added and the temperature is elevated to
80 ◦C. This results in Peroxydisulfuric acid, H2S2O8, which decomposes into H2SO5 and hydrogen
peroxide H2O2, both of which create free oxidising radicals. The strong acidic solution can also
remove alkali and metal traces like the RCA2. After this process W10 was left at this step for
potential future measurement. Based on the color change visible by eye, the wafer was deemed to
have successfully gone through the process steps. Another validation was done using a surface pro-
ﬁlometer, which is a DEKTAK XTA manufactured by Bruker. This system has a very sharp needle
(tip) which is moved across a surface with a very low force. It detects surface proﬁles by detecting
the vertical position of the tip. A proﬁle measurement is shown in Figure 6.4. The height of the
polySilicon plus the nitride layer is around 340 nm. The value should only be 320 nm according
to the ellipsometer measurements, so there is a discrepancy between the two measurements. This
might be due to local height variations in the layers and non-uniform deposition. There is a small
step on the right side of the bump, which was also seen on other bumps. This was not looked into
further. The small protrusion on the top is probably a small particle.
The other 9 wafers were also taken through the resist strip, the nitride etch and the wafer
cleaning after which they were subjected to a polySilicon strip. This was done for 90 seconds after
which the proﬁles were inspected in a microscope as seen in Figure 6.5. A color gradient can be

6.4
1st LOCOS process and bump deﬁnition
Recent considerations by Nanolab staﬀhas resulted in a maximum amount of continuous oxidation
time per run for high temperature. As both the furnace tube and boat to load the wafers were
==PAGE:81==
76
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.7: Cross sectional image captured using SEM
made of quartz, glass, which has a glass transition temperature around 1200 ◦C. Thus if the glass
is exposed to these high temperatures it will begin to deform with time. Thus a limit of 8 hours
processing at 1150 ◦C and 23 hours processing at 1100 ◦C was set by Nanolab. For deﬁnition of
the Si-bump, the height of the post oxide should be considered
The DREM trench ﬁlling with oxide was thus limited to around 3 µm of oxide growth. To
close a 2.5 µm wide trench, 1.25 µm should be grown from both sides. From section 2.6 at least
1.25 µm
0.56
= 2.23 µm needs to be grown. This would require at least 12.5 hours at 1100 ◦for the rate
constants estimated from the furnace script by PhD student Martin Lind Ommen at that point in
time. To be as certain of trench ﬁlling as possible, 23 hours oxidation time was chosen, giving an
oxide growth in the trench of around 3 µm. Simulations in Athena had not reached a point where
they were useful at this point in time, when this was decided. Also, stresses were not assumed to be
as big a problem as they would show to be. The problem was thought to be more directed towards
full ﬁlling of the trenches. 23 hours would also give a very thick post oxide in which would result
in a lower parasitic capacitance as shown from calculations in Section 2.3. The 23 hours was also
chosen based on the results from Bachelor student Mads Weile who did a project on the DREM
trenches with trench ﬁlling. He demonstrated that for an oxidation of 23 hours, the trenches would
be completely ﬁlled. In his work there was no problem from stresses, but the mask design was also
very diﬀerent from the one used in this project, i.e. it had a lower density of ﬁlled DREM etched
trenches.
An error was made in calculation of the bump height. As described in Chapter 2 the simulation
in Onscale, would also include an estimation of the vacuum gap for a pull-in potential of 200 V.
This vacuum gap was based on a structure with no nitride and oxide, thus, a vacuum gap of 375
nm was used. The 375 nm was also chosen as a compromise between the 3 cell radii on the wafers.
Taking into account the ratio of the volume expansion of oxide, the post oxide grown should
be:

77

where the parameters were introduced in Chapter 1. For tpost = 2.7µm hb =937 nm. This meant
that the oxide grown should be

This was wrong however, and later a COMSOL model made in the MEMS group was used to
ﬁnd the gap height g for a given pull-in potential. COMSOL is a Finite Eleent software well suited
for multiphysics. Without going too much in depth, the COMSOl model takes into account, the
eﬀective electrode area of the bump as well as the aluminum on top of the top plate. An air gap
200 nm would give a pull-in potential of 200 V for a cell radius of 22.5 µm.
Wafer 1-9 were then RCA cleaned before being loaded into the oxidation furnace tube and
subjected to a wet oxidation of 665 minutes at 1100 ◦C, with a post-anneal of 20 minutes. The
oxidation time was found using both the equations in section 2.6 and the furnace script. This
resulted in a thickness of 2097 nm, measured on the ellipsometer, which was a bit lower than
the estimated value of 2130 nm. This meant that the Si-bump should be around 0.44tox =924
nm instead of 937 nm. This gives a higher pull-in potential in the end, as the vacuum gap gets
larger, if the oxidation parameters are kept the same. But setting the oxidation time lower, if the
oxidation is not happening as fast as expected could result in an over correction for the second
LOCOS.
Directly after the 1st LOCOS, a wafer now called W9, was taken out of the batch, to be able
to do measurements on it. A surface proﬁle of this wafer can be seen in Figure 6.6. The step
height from the top of the oxide to the top of the nitride, should be around 950 nm, from the oxide
thickness estimated by the ellipsometer, but was only 830 nm. This could be due to oxidation of
Silicon nitride or that either instrument was wrong.
Some Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) images of these structures were also captured. W10
were cleaved and cross-section images of the LOCOS could then be captured. An overview of the
bump can be seen in Figure 6.7, where the light gray region is the Silicon, the darker grey is the
oxide, and the ’plate’ on top of those two materials are the nitride.
in Figure 6.7 the bird’s beak is clearly seen on both sides of the Silicon bump structure. If the
oxide and nitride are removed, only the Silicon bump structure will remain. The knobby surface
is caused by gold sputtering of the sample, which helps alleviate charging of the surface. Charging
in this sense is caused by electron being trapped in the surface, causing subsequent electrons to be
deﬂected and thus distorts the image. Taking a closer look in Figure 6.8a and 6.8b an asymmetry
in the bird’s beak from top to bottom of the oxide can be seen. The bottom part at the Silicon-
Oxide interface is more curved, whereas the Oxide-Nitride interface is ﬂatter. This means that the
nitride layer does have some rigidity, and that a stress-dependent viscous model should probably
be used during simulation, for thicker nitride layers. There is also some diﬀerence in the bending
of the nitride when comparing the two ﬁgures. This could be due to an uneven cleave, as it is very
hard to perfectly cleave the wafers by hand straight through the center of the structures. If there
is some gradient into the image the proﬁle might seem less steep than what it really is. Figures
6.8a and 6.8b might both be imperfectly cleaved. Using an image analysis tool called ImageJ, the
nitride thickness was estimated to be around 250 nm. This is a lot more than the 210 nm nitride
deposited and suggests that the nitride has indeed oxidised a bit. The height diﬀerence from the
top of the nitride in a ﬂat region to the top of the oxide was estimated to be between 850-900 nm,
which is between the value calculated and the surface proﬁle measurement.
It was later found that an error had been made in the calculations. The height of the pad
oxide was not included when calculating the Si-bump height and the oxidation time, so that the
height of the Si-bump was too low. This was partly found due to a more extensive analysis of the
SEM images. Another correction was of the oxide etch during the polySilicon strip. As the SEM
images were done after subsequent processing, this error was not corrected for. This correction
gave a predicted height diﬀerence between top of nitride to the top of the oxide of around 850
nm. In Table 6.1 an overview of the predicted an measured values is shown. From the table the
discrepancy in predicted and measured nitride thickness and in the step height from nitride to to
top of the oxide is more clear. This further suggests the oxidation of nitride.

As described before, the nitride can oxidise, and thus before stripping the nitride in a nitride
etch bath, an oxide etch is done. This is done using BHF, which is a buﬀered HF solution which
is 12 % HF containg ammonium ﬂuoride, which ensures that the etch rate remains constant and is
slower than for concentrated HF. The etch rate is approximately 75-80 nm/min. The 8 remaining
wafers were dipped in the BHF solution for 3 minutes and rinsed in DI-water afterwards. The
nitride was then stripped by submerging the wafers in the nitride etch bath for 105 minutes. The

Some of the bumps have been damaged as seen in Figure 6.10a. This could be due to a number
of diﬀerent issues; If the nitride ﬁlm had particles in it, pinholes might be introduced resulting
in the incomplete deﬁnition of the bump. If particles were present on the mask, then some resist
might have been unexposed resulting in the etching of the layers i.e. the bumps. In Figure 6.10b
the remaining alignment mark is shown. Here can be seen that the Vernier structures are not well
separated. As there is some deﬁnition of the individual lines they were usable however. For future
design, the lines should be made thinner for them to be more well-deﬁned.

6.5
Isolation oxide, nitride and polySilicon deposition and
cavity structure deﬁnition
For clarity the second LOCOS process with a ﬁlled DREM etched trench is restated her with the
following list with corresponding Figure 6.11:
(K1) Using the ’Cavity’ mask a nitride oxidation barrier is deﬁned for the cavities. Using the
’DREM’ mask, an opening into the isolation oxide is made.
(L1) A trench is etch through the opening using the DREM etch.
(M1) 2nd LOCOS process is performed deﬁning post oxide and ﬁlling the trench. (wafer batch 1
was stopped at this step)
(N1) polySOI wafer is fusion bonded to the substrate wafer
(O1) The polySilicon on the backside is removed, the ’Box’ oxides are removed and the handle
layer is removed.
(P1) Aluminum is deposited
(Q1) The wafer is etched/lapped(grinded) from the backside separating the elements. As shown
in O2) and P2) contact have also been made to the bottom, but is made inside every single
element if not strictly connected from the backside.
After stripping the oxide a new pad oxide was to be oxidised for the cavity structures. This pad
oxide also serves the purpose of extra electrical insulation against short-curcuiting of the top plate
and the substrate. Therefore this oxide needs to be thicker than the previous oxide. The desired
thickness was 400 nm. Wafers 2-8 were RCA cleaned and afterwards oxidised in the furnace for 8
hours and 20 minutes in a dry atmosphere at 1100 ◦C. A test wafer was included and placed in
the center of the boat as in the previous oxidations. This wafer had an oxide thickness of 401.5
nm after processing.
Wafers 2-8 was then loaded directly into the nitride furnace, which had been cleaned for particles
in the meantime. A test wafer was included and placed in the center. 3 Dummy wafers were placed
in the outermost spots for a more uniform deposition, in case white particles were blown through
the system again. The deposition was run for 55 minutes, which was a little less than the previous
run as the previous layer was too thick. Afterwards the test wafer was measured on the ellipsometer.
The nitride thickness was measured to be 202.4 nm.
Wafers 2-8 were then directly loaded into the polySilicon furnace according to the loading
procedure.
polySilicon was deposited for 12 minutes and 30 seconds.
Again the layer in the
==PAGE:86==
6.5. ISOLATION OXIDE, NITRIDE AND POLYSILICON DEPOSITION AND CAVITY STRUCTURE DEFINITIO
Figure 6.11: Overview schematic of the 2nd LOCOS processes and bonding afterwards used in this
batch of fabrication. The steps are explained in the list above the ﬁgure.
previous run had been too thick and this was adjusted in the deposition time. The test wafer was
measured on the Filmtek and had a polySilicon layer thickness of 101 nm.
After these steps, the wafers were spin-coated with a 1.5 µm layer of AZ nLOF 2020 resist
including an HMDS priming step in the Spin Coater: Gamma UV machine. At this point in time
streak lines were seen on a single wafer after the spin-coating step which suggested that the bumps
aﬀected the spin-coating. Streak lines are usually caused by particles on the wafer or in the resist,
and are the result of the ﬂow over this particles - like water ﬂowing around an object. This was
erroneously deemed not signiﬁcant, as the thickness of the resist was still thicker than the bumps
were tall and the other wafers seemed to not have any streak lines. This would later cause large
errors in the structures.
The wafers were then exposed for 10.2 seconds under 365 nm wavelength light with a constant
intensity of 11 mW/cm2. The ’Cavity’ mask was used and alignment was done to the ﬁrst layers
alignment marks. From the alignment procedure, it was deemed necessary to go into contact and
checking the alignment before exposing. Usually the alignment is done with a certain spacing
between substrate and mask. After aligning, the mask and substrate goes into contact and the
substrate is exposed. The machine can however go into contact without exposing, so that alignment
can be checked. This gives better alignment. Directly after they were post exposure baked for 60
seconds at 110 ◦C, and then developed using a TMAH puddle on the wafer for 60 seconds. The
alignment marks were inspected on a single wafer, which did not show any real problem with the
resist, other than a small misalignment.
After this procedure wafer 2-8 were submerged in polySilicon etch solution for 7 minutes, spaced
in every 2nd slot in the etching carrier. The increase in time was due to rinsing of the carrier holder.
After this step the wafers were placed into their wafer box and put on a storage shelf. The next
process was to strip the resist in the plasma asher. As the wafers were retrieved from the shelf a
==PAGE:87==
82
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.12: Alignment of the DREM mask to the cavity mask.
lapse in concentration, caused the wafer box to hit against the shelf and the box was dropped on
the ground. The wafers partly rolled out, only touching the ground on the sides and were mixed
to some degree. Some wafers fell to the ground. To try and save as many wafers as possible, most
were scooped back into the wafer box, scratching against each other. The wafers were then sorted,
though the wafers at the same process step were indiscernible making numbering obsolete. All
22 wafers were then subjected to plasma ashing for an hour which should remove a lot of organic
material, which could have been introduced. This would also strip the resist. Furthermore, the 22
wafers were subjected to a wafer clean in the 7-up solution for 13 minutes to further remove organic
material. The wafers that had a pad oxide of around 100 nm seemed to be scratched, whereas the
other wafers seemed ﬁne. Wafer 2-8 fortunately had the polySilicon protected by the resist. The
nitride exposed at this point was to be etched anyway, and so processing was continued.
The wafers 2-8 were then submerged into a nitride etch bath for 92 minutes at 160 ◦C to etch
the nitride layer of 202.4 nm. Afterwards W2-5 had the polySilicon stripped in a KOH 28 %
solution for 2 minutes at 40 ◦C. The KOH etch selectivity for Si/SiO2 goes from around 250 at
80 ◦C to 750 at 40 ◦C [32]. The etch rate at 40 ◦C 0.1 µm/min meaning that 2 minutes should
be an over etch of the 100 nm polySilicon. The amount of oxide etched should only be around
3 Angstroms. The polySilicon is not stripped in the polySilicon etch solution due to poorer etch
selectivity. The KOH might however introduce alkali ions in the oxide, which is undesired as they
might cause surface states to be available for charging. This will be explained more in depth in
the characterisation chapter. After the nitride etch and the KOH, the wafers were cleaned in the
wafer cleaning solution 7-up.
Wafers 6-8 had their polySilicon stripped using a dry etching system. Most dry etching systems
at Nanolab’s cleanroom is Inductively Coupled Plasma etchers. The ICP’s have a decoupled power
curcuit, one for generating the plasma, and one for accelerating reactive species unto the surface of
the substrates. They use the principle of (deep) reactive ion etching in which etchant species are
adsorbed unto a surface, forming volatile products with the surface material, and then desorbed
and pumped away. The ICP’s generate the plasma at 13.56 MHz using a coil that is decoupled from
==PAGE:88==
6.6. DREM TRENCH DEFINITION AND ETCH
83
Figure 6.13: Alignment of the Cavity mask to the DREM mask.
the substrate to obtain a high density plasma. The main diﬀerence in the ICP’s at the Nanolab
cleanroom facility is the gases supplied, i.e.
the etch chemistries available, and the substrates
allowed in the diﬀerent machines. A 2 cycle bosch process with 12 seconds etching was used to
strip the polySilicon that has been designed for shallow etches, in the ICP called DRIE Pegasus.
The chuck the wafers were clamped to was set to have a temperature of 20 ◦C degrees which is the
standard temperature of the system. At this point the wafers were coveren with isolation oxide,
and had a nitride mask that would deﬁne the cavities in the 2nd LOCOS.
6.6
DREM trench deﬁnition and etch
Wafer 2-5 were then spin coated with 1.5 µm thick AZ MiR701 positive resist including an HMDS
priming step. No streak lines were observed. After this they were exposed for 18 seconds under 365
nm wavelength light with a constant intensity of 11 mW/cm2. The exposure was done using the
’DREM’ mask. As described in Chapter 4, no open structures with an opening larger than 2.5 µm
could be made in the design. This made the alignment very diﬃcult as almost no light could pass
through these openings. To approximately align the structures, the alignment separation distance
was moved from the standard 30 µm to 10 µm. Also, it was crucial to go into contact alignment
check to check alignment before exposing. The alignment of the DREM etch mask to the cavity
can be seen in Figure 6.12 and the alignment of the cavity mask to the bump pattern can be seen
in Figure 6.13. For the DREM alignment is seen that it has been well aligned with a small up-right
misalignment. For the cavity mask can be seen that it has been misaligned slightly.
Before being able to perform the DREM etch, the isolation oxide had to be removed in the
desired regions. For this, the ICP called AOE was used. The chuck, which the wafers are clamped
to in the system, was set to 0 ◦C degrees, with a C4F8 and H2 gas chemistry. This etches oxide at
a rate of around 200 nm/min. The etching was done for 3 minutes on wafers 2-5.
Afterwards the wafers 2-5 were subjected to a DREM etch. As described in Chapter 2 this is
a modiﬁed Bosch process including a break step that sputters oﬀthe horizontal layer of deposited
==PAGE:89==
84
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
(a) Top of DREM trench.
(b) Bottom of DREM trench.
Figure 6.14: Images taken by a SEM of the bumps after an altered DREM process and trench ﬁll
by TEOS oxide deposition. The images were taken by Mads Alexander Weile.
material. The speciﬁc recipe used in this project was developed in the special course project by
bachelor student Mads Weile in collaboration with Bingdong Chang. The chuck temperature was
set to -19 ◦C and 308 cycles were used. The etching time step was ramped through the recipe
from 2.5 s to 6 s. The process was meant to create a V-shape, but this happened mostly at the
bottom of the trench. This is shown in Figure 6.14 by the trench width diﬀerence from the top to
the bottom going from 3 µm to 1.5 µm.

The wafers then had the resist stripped in the plasma asher for 60 minutes, with standard
process parameters. They were then cleaned using the RCA, but without the HF dip steps, as
the oxide should not be removed. The wafers 2-3 were then oxidised for 23 hours at 1100 ◦C and
annealed for 20 minutes. This should result in a post oxide with a thickness of 3050 nm. The
oxide thickness was measured on a test wafer without isolation oxide to be 3029 nm compared
with the expected thickness of 3030 nm. This meant that the post oxide should be around the
desired thickness of 3050 nm. The wafers also had a visible wafer bow after being oxidised. As
this hindered clamping using vacuum or other methods in the machines used for processing, this
eﬀectively stopped the current process at this step.
It had not been documented before and
therefore was not accounted for in either the mask design or in the process. As bachelor student
Mads A. Weile had done a trench ﬁll using oxidation for the same amount of time, the process
was expected to succeed. However, the mask design he had used, did not have the same density
of trench ﬁlled lines.
Afterwards the wafers were characterised using a surface proﬁlometer and using the optical
microscope. An example of the surface proﬁle after the 2nd LOCOS process can be seen in Figure
6.15. As a reference the surface proﬁle just before the second LOCOS is also shown in Figure 6.15.
Around the 20 µm mark is the DREM trench opening seen as the small downwards spike. This
shape is caused by the needle having a dimension larger than the DREM etch opening. Before
the second LOCOS a step of approximately 200 nm can be seen, which is the nitride layer. After
the oxidation the oxide has grown around the trench as expected and the nitride has been bent
upwards. Going to the 40 µm mark a large plateau can be seen after the LOCOS. Comparing to
the pre-LOCOS proﬁle, this is the edge of the Si-bump. This means that the corner of the bump
has oxidised. Between the two plateau’s the nitride layer seems intact, and is in fact at a lower
height than the post oxide around the DREM trench opening. The right hill is the post oxide
between two cells, and can be seen to be more ﬂat, than the region around the DREM trench.
This post oxide also sits higher than around the trench.
In Figure 6.16 and 6.17 microscope images of the cells after the 2nd LOCOS can be seen. In
Figure 6.16 is seen the bump as the smallest which seem to have an non-ﬂat surface. Around the
bump there is a pinkish ring with a color gradient from the mid section of the ring to the outer
==PAGE:91==
86
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.16: Microscope image after the second LOCOS process with no DREM etch opening for
the plate separation structures on M8. The many rings indicate process failure.
section. This is probably the nitride layer, which has been bent upwards, that causes the color
gradient. In between the bump and the ring, which is the slope region of the Si-bump, oxidation
has also happened which gives rise to the plateaus. In Figure 6.17 another case of this can be seen.
it has the same pattern, but the top part of bump has a crescent moon-shaped oxidised structure.
This is the same cells as the one shown in Figure 6.15. This is probably a point where the nitride
ﬁlm has been very thin and thus the oxidation has happened further into the bump. Interestingly,
this is the bottom right corner of array M9, and the crescent shapes lies in a direction that is
almost perpendicular to the center of the wafer. Thus it was suspected that the resist had been
too thin. As the resist is spun unto the wafer, it ﬂows outwards from the center. If it meets an
obstacle, i.e. the bumps, the ﬂow will either go above or around them. If the ﬂow is too high, it
will be propelled over the top, not properly covering the side opposite to the ﬂow direction. During
subsequent etches the thinner, or missing resist layer, will allow material to be etched in undesired
regions.
To further investigate the structures, scanning electron microscopy was used. A top view of
cells in one of the arrays on the left side can be seen in Figure 6.18 and tilted views can be seen
in Figure 6.19a and 6.19b. In the overview, the crescent shape can be seen to the left side of all
bumps. Edge lifting can also be seen around the bumps. A small curvature towards the DREM
trench opening can also be seen faintly. In Figure 6.19a the measurements show that the cavity
diameter is close to the desired value of 62 µm. As the bump should be around 41 µm in diameter
for this cell, it further validates that it is indeed the edge of the bump that has been oxidised.
Looking at Figure 6.19b it seems as if the nitride has almost cracked open along the edge of the
bump, if not completely etched.
Figure 6.20 and 6.21 shows cross-sectional images of the cells after the 2nd LOCOS process,
with and without measurements. The thickness of the oxide layer in the trench is approximately
the same as on the test wafer, meaning that it has continued to grow, even after the oxide fronts
have met, as the oxide thickness should have been around 2.23 µm. This could potentially induce
==PAGE:92==
6.7. TEST OF DREM ETCH AND TRENCH FILLING
87
Figure 6.17: Microscope image after the second LOCOS process of array M9 with a DREM etch

the stresses seen from the wafer bow. A lot small voids in the oxide can be seen. They seem to
have been closed oﬀperiodically, which should not allow any gas ﬂow into the trench. There is a
signiﬁcant distance from the trench opening to the top of the oxide as also seen in Figure 5.14 in
Chapter 5 which is 11.64 µm. Comparing Figure 6.21 to the simulation results (Figure 5.14); The
slope of the oxide proﬁle in the SEM image seems to be small but reaches very far into the element
as the simulation also shows. The maximum point lies very close to the edge of the nitride layer,
as was also seen in the simulation. In these cross-sectional images it is also clear how the bump
protrudes higher than the post oxide, and that the nitride layer does not cover the bump.
Taking a look at the trench ﬁll in Figure 6.22 and 6.23; The trenches are both around 100 µm
in depth, and seem to have been ﬁlled all the way to the bottom, though there is a line at the
oxide fronts. In ﬁgure 6.23 there is a more profound V-shape in the proﬁle, as the width at the
bottom of the trench is signiﬁcantly smaller 4.5 µm compared to 5.5 µm.
In Figure 6.24 is shown the horizontal stress induced by oxide growth at diﬀerent oxidation
time steps. Figure 6.24a shows the oxidation just as the oxide fronts meet, and there are almost
no stresses induced.
After oxidising a bit more, the trench is almost completely ﬁlled, and a
signiﬁcant amount of stress induced, being highest at the bottom of the trench and propagating
upwards. After a signiﬁcant amount of time, the stress has further increased but has been alleviated
slightly. The trench has not become signiﬁcantly thicker however and is under 5 µm at all times,
which is conﬂicting with the SEM cross-sectional images.
6.7
Test of DREM etch and trench ﬁlling
To test whether the wafer bow was exclusively caused by the trench ﬁlling, a DREM etch test was
set up. 6 new wafers were bought, with the same speciﬁcations as in batch 1. These wafers were
only subjected to the DREM etch with the same DREM mask as the one used in batch 1. They
==PAGE:93==
88
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.18: SEM image after the second LOCOS process with a DREM etch opening separating
the elements. The Si-bump has a ring on the edge, which is the oxidised region. The crescent shapes
perpendicular to the center of the wafer can also be seen indicating a failure in the spin-coating.
(a) SEM image after the second LOCOS process
with a tilted view and measurements.
