were spin-coated, exposed and developed in the same way. After Development they were inspected
using an optical microscope to verify the width of the opening in the resist as seen in Figure 6.25.
Here it is shown that the width is 2.63 µm according to the measurement, which is comparable to
the designed width of 2.5 µm.
The wafers were then DREM etched using the same DREM etch recipe as before, and a wafer
was inspected in the optical microscope as shown in Figure 6.26. Here the trench width is approx-
==PAGE:94==
6.8. OPTIMISATION OF SPIN COATING
89
Figure 6.20: SEM cross-sectional image after the second LOCOS process with measurements.
imately 3-3.5 µm, but it is tough to gauge whether this is the actual value of the opening, as the
etch of the resist has been attacked by the etch, and the trench traps all light.
Afterwards, 2 wafers were left un-oxidised, 4 were oxidised for 11 hours and annealed for 20
minutes. 2 of the wafer were then taken out of the furnace and 2 wafers were oxidised further 12
hours including 20 minutes annealing. The wafers that had been oxidised for a total of 23 hours
had a visible wafer bow as before. A photo of the visible wafer bow is shown in Figure 6.27
To alleviate the stress problem several solutions was discussed. One solution could be to oxidize
for a shorter amount of time. The width of the oxide in the ﬁlled DREM trench was larger than 5
µm as the simulation showed to be the maximum width. Thus extra material has been squeezed
into an already ﬁlled region, potentially causing stress. By oxidizing less, this would not happen
to the same degree. However, the trench should still be ﬁlled so some optimization would probably
be necessary for a successful fabrication. Another solution was to alleviate stresses by creating
openings on the local and macroscale. If a 5 µm wide opening was made around every array, they
would be able to relieve some stress by expanding into this opening. Local openings in the element
separation wider than 2.5 µm could also be made so that the oxide expand into these regions.
Finally, it was shown in [6], that by periodically applying an isotropic etch in-between DREM etch
steps, small openings could be made vertically. By optimizing these possibilities a scaﬀold of oxide
could be made in the DREM trench.
6.8
Optimisation of spin coating
As discussed in the Section 6.6, the spin coating was probably the cause of error of the bumps being
oxidised. To test this hypothesis, experiments were done on wafer 1 which was left at the step just
before the isolation oxide was grown, meaning it only had bumps. The Wafer was ﬁrst spin-coated
with a 1.5 µm thick AZ nLOF 2020 resist. Then it was examined under an optical microscope and
surface proﬁled. The resist was stripped in the plasma asher using the standard recipe parameters.
Afterwards it was coated with 4 µm thick AZ nLOF 2020 resist and characterised the same way.
==PAGE:95==
90
CHAPTER 6. FABRICATION - 1ST BATCH
Figure 6.21: SEM image after the second LOCOS process without measurements to better see the
proﬁle.
Figure 6.28 shows microscope images of 1.5 µm and 4 µm resist on the M5 array. For 1.5 µm
resist thickness, there are visible rings around the bumps. In the center of the bump there is a
red dot and a similar color in the space between the bumps. This shows how the resist has ﬂown
around the bumps, but has not properly covered the bumps. For 4 µm resist thickness, there is
still a red color between the bumps, but it is much less visible, and his only present where the
elements should be separated. Within the elements, there is a very good almost uniform color.
Figure 6.29 shows resist thickness 1.5 µm and 4 µm for the M9 array. In the case of 1.5 µm
the coverage is very bad and there are multiple rings on and around the bumps. The ﬂow has
been very much aﬀected by the Si-bumps, and even more so at this array at the edge of the wafer,
compared to M5 which sits at the center. There are also visible streak lines to the side of the array
showing how the ﬂow has propagated, where the array seems to have been a barrier for the resist
ﬂow. For 4 µm there is a much more uniform color distribution, but the there still seem to be a
sign of non complete coverage towards the end of the array.
The M9 array was also investigated using the surface proﬁler, and Figure 6.30 and 6.31 shows
the proﬁle for 1.5 and 4 µm thick resist respectively. In Figure 6.30 the bump is visible through
the resist, as the structure is very tall. Irregularities in coating can be seen over the bump. In
Figure 6.30 shows a much better coverage and the hill has a much smaller height compared to 1.5
µm thick layer of resist. Noting that there should be approximately 4 µm resist, the coverage was
deemed to be good enough, given the much smaller height variation.
6.9
Summary
In this chapter, the processing of the ﬁrst batch of wafers was presented. The motivation behind the
diﬀerent process steps was explained. Potential oxidation of nitride was observed during inspection
of the 1st LOCOS process, using several characterization methods. After the 2nd LOCOS, the
wafers had a visible wafer bow, which inhibited further processing. As several problems was present
==PAGE:96==
6.9. SUMMARY
91
Figure 6.22: SEM image of the ﬁlled trench after the second LOCOS process.
on the wafers, the wafer bow was veriﬁed by a just making the DREM etched trench and ﬁlling
it by oxidation, resulting in a wafer bow again. It was hypothesized that the wafer bow was due
to stress. Several solutions was proposed to solve this problem. One was to oxidize for a shorter
period of time. Another was to make some kind of stress-relieving structure, for example by making
large openings around the arrays so they could locally expand. Spin coating after the 1st LOCOS
was also found to be a problem, when using 1.5 µm thick resist. This resulted in non-complete
nitride coverage of the bumps and there the edges of the bumps were oxidized. The problem was
potentially solved by applying a 4µm thick resist.

Chapter 7
Fabrication - 2nd Batch
After the errors run into during the processing of batch 1 several options for the future path of the
project could be made;
1. Process a second batch of wafers by using the process ﬂow of a conventional double LOCOS
with top plate element separation for a potentially higher success rate than with the full
process integration.
2. Process a second batch of wafers where the elements were separated by a non-ﬁlled DREM
gap and bonded on both sides.
3. Continue with the current batch to try and ﬁx it, by solving several process related problems
at once.
4. Isolated solutions for the DREM trench ﬁlling problem could be investigated.
Through discussions with the other members of the group, but mainly with the supervisors of
this thesis, it was decided that the ’safest possible’ double LOCOS should be made (option 1) with
the same masks. Thus the designed parameters could be tested, which had also been a signiﬁcant
part of the project. A larger emphasis was put on documenting the work step-by-step in the second
batch to better catch any errors. This was more feasible, as authorisation to use the equipment
unaccompanied had been gained for most processes. Second in priority was to investigate solutions
for the DREM trench ﬁlling (option 4), but only if time allowed it.
The reason not to pick option 3, was that if it failed, there would not be time for a second batch
of the double LOCOS. The wafers had also been dropped on the ﬂoor which could potentially mess
up processing and CMUT performance. Furthermore, the nitride layer would have to be etched
and redeposited to structure it succesfully.
This chapter will ﬁrst go through the processing for bottom substrate wafers - the ones that
are structured with double LOCOS, then the processing of the PSOI’s will be presented, and lastly
the processing for the bonded pair of wafers will be explained.
For clarity the process overview for the 1st LOCOS is restated in the following list corresponding
to Figure 7.1.
(A) A layer of oxide is grown. Then nitride is deposited. Then polySilicon is deposited. Finally
(photo)resist is spin-coated. The resist will only be displayed in these ﬁrst three steps.
(B) By using the mask ’Bump’, the Si-bump pattern is deﬁned in the resist
(C) The polySilicon is etched transferring the pattern to the polySilicon layer.
(D) Resist is stripped.
(E) The pattern is transferred to the nitride layer by etching the nitride.
(F) PolySilicon is stripped
98
==PAGE:104==
7.1. NEW WAFERS AND DEPOSITION OF LAYERS FOR 1ST LOCOS
99
Figure 7.1: Overview of the ﬁrst LOCOS process in a schematic. The steps are explained in the
list above the ﬁgure.
(G) 1st LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) process
(H) Nitride is stripped
(I) Oxide is stripped
(J) A layer of oxide is grown, then nitride is deposited and ﬁnally polySilicon is deposited for the
2nd LOCOS process.
The process ﬂow for Batch 2 is found in Appendix C where the process has been modiﬁed to
accommodate the results of this chapter.
7.1
New wafers and deposition of layers for 1st LOCOS
25 n-type (Antimony) doped wafers with the lowest resistivity available(<0.025 Ωcm were bought.
The wafers were the same kind as bought in the ﬁrst batch as it was still important that they had
a low resistivity, because the substrate would be used as a ground electrical connection. All of the
wafers were single side polished, SSP, which was a mistake. This would make the later processing
a little more cumbersome. SSP wafer are 525 µm in thickness whereas the double side polished,
DSP, wafers are only 350 µm in thickness.
==PAGE:105==
100
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION - 2ND BATCH
All of the wafers were RCA cleaned, with HF dips after RCA1 and RCA2 steps. This was okay
since there was no initial oxide and the wafers would go straight into the furnace. 14 of the wafers
were then oxidised for 88 minutes at 1050 ◦in a dry atmosphere. The processing of the other 11
wafers is continued in Section 7.5. This oxidation resulted in an oxide thickness of 100 nm as
desired. Directly after the oxidation, stoichiometric nitride was deposited on the 14 wafers for 55
minutes in the nitride furnace. As the wafers came out, particles and a non-uniform deposition
were spotted on the wafers placed outermost in the furnace boat. 55 minutes resulted in a layer
of 199 nm nitride, measured using the Ellipsometer. This is close to the desired value of 200 nm.
polySilicon was then deposited for 12 minutes and 30 seconds. The measured thickness of the layer
using the FilmTek was 99 nm, close to the 100 nm desired. As the layers generate diﬀerent colors
and thicknesses during processing this can also be used as a visual check that the processing has
succeeded. This was also done to some degree during the ﬁrst batch, but in the second batch it
was more thorough. The color resulting from the deposition of the oxide, nitride and polySilicon
can be seen in Figure 7.2.

7.2
Deﬁnition of nitride and 1st LOCOS
After the polySilicon deposition one wafer was deemed to have too nonuniform deposition and too
many particles to continue processing. An attempt at removing the particles was done using the
plasma ashing for 60 minutes, but the particles were not removed and the wafer were exempted
from further processing. The 13 other wafer were then spin-coated with 1.5 µm AZ nLOF 2020

101
resist including HMDS priming. The ’Bumps’ mask was then used in hard contact mode, and the
wafers were exposed to 11 mW/cm2 for 10.2 seconds. They were then puddle developed in TMAH
for 60 seconds after having been post-exposure baked for 60 seconds. After the development, wavy
patterns were seen on the photoresist that was left on the wafer. This also happened when deﬁning
the cavities later in the process and an example is shown in Figure 7.3.
Figure 7.3: Image of wafer showing wavy patterns in the photoresist after development both during

A wafer was photographed with a camera and in optical microscope as shown in Figure 7.4. The
microscope software can change the color scale depending on the white reference. The reference
was chosen to be a white tissue, to get as reliable colors as possible. A surface proﬁle measurement
of the resist layer was also done and found to be between 1.4 and 1.5 µm as intended. Surface proﬁle
measurements was used to estimate patterning height diﬀerences for all processing measurements
unless otherwise stated.
The resist bumps were measured to have a diameter on array M5 of
approximately 29 µm in diameter using imageJ, a free image analyzing software.
After deﬁning the resist, the polySilicon were etched on wafer 1 in the polySilicon etch solution
for 6 minutes, to test if the solution etch rate had gone signiﬁcantly down. The wafer was inspected
by photograph and by microscope. The images are shown in Figure 7.5. The wafer has clearly
changed color in non-patterned regions both by eye, and in the microscope image. The diameter
of the bump was measure to be approximately 30 µm using imageJ. Thus the polySilicon had
very likely been etched, because the uniformity in color and the change of height in the proﬁle.
Bumps on the array M5 was measured to be around 30 µm in diameter. The height variations on
a non-structured surface over 3 mm were around 35 nm. They should be less than around 100 nm
according to Equation 5.2 over a distance of 1 mm.
Afterwards, the single wafer was plasma ashed using the standard parameters. The result is
shown in Figure 7.6, where a color change in the arrays is faintly as a lighter shade of the previous
color. The height was 100-105 nm and the bump in array R9 was around 41 µm. The height
variations over 2 mm were around 20 nm. Thus the rest of the wafers (2-13) were processed in the
same way spaced 1 cm apart in the carrier boats.
After stripping the resist the wafers were etched in the nitride etch solution for 91 minutes at
160 ◦C degrees spaced 1 cm apart, and were afterwards wafer cleaned in the 7-up etch solution.
The result is shown in Figure 7.7. The areas outside the arrays have clearly changed color from

Several checks could be made at this point.
If water runs oﬀ
cleanly, the wafer is hydrophobic meaning pure Silicon was left, which the water did. The wafer
should be completely gray, which it was as shown in Figure 7.11. To characterize the bumps at
==PAGE:110==
7.2. DEFINITION OF NITRIDE AND 1ST LOCOS
105
this point, 2 diameters were measured. All the bumps have a black circle around them, which is
where light is not reﬂected back into the microscope as seen of Figure 7.11b. This means either
a signiﬁcant change in height of the structure or high absorption. As it is only Silicon it must
be a change in height, and so it signiﬁes the sloped region of the bump as described in Chapter
1. The inner diameter is the diameter on the inside of the sloped region, and the outer diameter,
is outside the sloped region. On the array M5, the designed diameter was 30 µm. The outer
diameter was measured to be 30 µm and the inner to be 23.5 µm. On the array L9 the designed
diameter was 41.33 µm, the inner is 35 µm and the outer is 41 µm. The height of the Silicon bumps
were measured to be 960 nm using the surface proﬁler. The designed bump height was 1 µm, but
the diﬀerence was primarily due to an error in calculations of the oxidation time. Correcting the
calculation, gave a projected height of 956 nm. Then the designed post oxide height should be
corrected to 2.42 µm instead of 2.5 µm.
(a) Photograph of wafer.
(b) Optical microscope image of wafer.
Figure 7.11: Wafer progress after stripping the residual oxide grown in the 1st LOCOS step.
Before growing the isolation oxide, the wafer roughness were estimated using an Atomic Force
Microscope (AFM). An AFM is a surface proﬁling tool, like the DEKTAK, but trades xyz resolution
for scan range. It is thus more useful when dealing with sub micron to micron sized structures.
The needle either gently taps the surface, runs along the surface in contact or can use a mode
where it predicts the vicinity of the surface using surface force calculations. The last mode was
used. The needle scans in a trace-retrace manner. This means it scans forward and then ’retraces’
(oing back along the same path. It then moves a small step down in the perpendicular direction
to make another trace-retrace scan. The image processing of the AFM scan were done in a free
software called Gwyddion. All scans were subjected to 3 diﬀerent ﬁlters. The ﬁrst was to level the
underlying plane of the 2D scan. The second was to move the bottom point of the scan height to
0. The third was to use a row aligning tool to level the trace-retrace curves to all having the same
slope. This slope method is called polynomial row aligning of 1st order. When doing ﬁltering of
physical measurements, one must be wary of eliminating actual physical features. Sometimes it
can be necessary however, due to the equipment’s performance on day-to-day, sample-to-sample
basis, which is why these ﬁlters were run. The plane levelling should account for the wafer having
a slight tilt, and the row aligning should account for the errors induced by moving the needle
rapidly in trace-retrace. ISO 25178 parameters of the surface roughness is shown in Table 7.1 for
a scan range of 0.5 µm by 0.5 µm and 5 µm by 5 µm in regions that are not patterned. The RMS
roughness (Sq) is below 0.5 nm and the maximum height diﬀerence is below 5 nm across a 5 µm2
region. Wafers can be fusion bonded if the roughness is below 0.5 nm [26], [5], meaning that the
roughness was still low enough after the ﬁrst half of the processing.
The wafers were subjected to another BHF dip of 3 minutes as there were some residual oxide
left in the region where the wafers had been covered by the carrier boat.

7.3
Deposition of layers for 2nd LOCOS
After this step 8 wafers were RCA cleaned and oxidised for 8 hours and 20 minutes in a dry
atmosphere at 1100 ◦C. This resulted in an oxide thickness of 402 nm, which was measured by
an ellipsometer on a test wafer. Doing it in a dry atmosphere serves another purpose than being
able to control the thickness of the oxide better. The oxidation is less uniform as the radius of
curvature of structures become smaller[18]. The smaller the radius the lower the oxidation rate,
and the lower the temperature, the more pronounced this eﬀect is, especially for dry oxidation.
This means that the proﬁle of the bump becomes sharper than if it had been a wet oxidation. This
is similar to results from the Athena simulations in Chapter 5, where it was seen that the proﬁle
became smoother with increasing temperature.
The nitride furnace had been cleaned prior to this next nitride deposition step due to the particle
issue described earlier. The ﬁrst quality control (QC) run had given a particle count of around
6000 over a whole wafer. For a completely new wafer it is under 10. Thus only 4 wafers were chosen
to be processed further as the nitride deposition might deposit to many particles. With 4 wafers,
a lot of dummy wafers could also be placed in the boat, maybe shielding the 4 wafers better. The
4 wafers were then processed for 55 minutes in the nitride furnace. The test wafer included were
ﬁrst measured on a particle counter, with the same settings as the QC and the result is displayed
in Figure 7.12.
Figure 7.12: Image of a scan of the nitride deposition test wafer showing the distribution and
amount of particles after nitride deposition.
Here, a count of only 74 particles is detected, much less than the 6000. Another issue during
==PAGE:112==
7.3. DEPOSITION OF LAYERS FOR 2ND LOCOS
107
these fabrication runs were that the RCA cleaning had actually been shown to introduce particles,
so that there were more particles than on new wafers, even without any prior processing. The
measurements on the RCA cleaning particle deposition was done by Majken Becker, a Nanolab
employee, and a full RCA was shown to have deposited around 70 particles, with the same settings
on the particle counter as the QC run. Thus 74 particles was a small number since the test wafers
are sitting in a box that has been opened a few times, not always under laminar air ﬂow from HEPA
ﬁlters. HEPA ﬁlters purify the air from 99.9 percent of particles in ambient air. The number of
particles were deemed small enough to continue processing. The test wafer nitride layer thickness
was measured on the ellipsometer to be 199 nm.
After a few days, where another cleanroom user had used the nitride LPCVD furnace, the other
4 wafers were going to be processed similarly. When the furnace had opened particles were seen
all over the inside of the furnace and dummy wafers as shown in Figure 7.13a and 7.7b, and thus
it was decided not to continue processing of the rest of the wafers.

7.4
Deﬁnition of 2nd LOCOS mask layer
For clarity the overview of the steps following the 1st LOCOS are listed in the following with
corresponding Figure 7.15.
(K2) Using the ’Cavity’ mask a nitride oxidation barrier is deﬁned for the cavities.
(L2) 2nd LOCOS process is performed deﬁning post oxide and cavity
(M2) polySOI wafer is fusion bonded to the substrate wafer.
(N2) The polySilicon on the backside is removed, the ’Box’ oxides are removed and the handle
layer is removed.
(O2) Using the ’Etch to bottom’ mask, the top plate and post oxide is etched to electrically contact
the substrate
(P2) Aluminum is deposited
(Q2) Aluminium and polySilicon top plate is etched to electrically separate the elements.
From Chapter 6 it was learned that the resist layer should be 4 µm thick. So 4 µm thick AZ
nLOF 2020 was spun on the wafers with HMDS priming. The wafers were then exposed with
the ’Cavity’ mask in hard contact mode with a light intensity of 11 mW/cm2 for 20 seconds and
afterwards developed in the same way as in the previous development procedures. A photograph
during the alignment check is shown in Figure 7.16c, where the Vernier marks and the circles can
be used to gauge alignment. The Vernier marks are especially useful during the alignment check,
as it gives a very precise indication of alignment and how much it should be moved when not in
the alignment check contact mode. Figure 7.16a and 7.16b shows that the pattern has transferred
(in the alignment) well after etching the diﬀerent layers.

7.5
PSOI wafer processing
Before moving on to the bonding and post-bonding processing, the fabrication of the PolySilicon-
On-Insulator (PSOI), will be run through. The fabrication process was developed by Andreas
Spandet Havreland, a Postdoc in the MEMS group at DTU.
The overall process is that Silicon wafers are oxidized to create a box oxide layer. PolySilicon is
then deposited either doped during deposition or afterwards, for a low resistivity. The polySilicon
is then polished to get the desired surface roughness.
After an RCA cleaning 11 wafers were oxidised for 8 hours and 20 minutes in a dry atmosphere
at 1100 ◦C degrees to get an oxide thickness of 400 nm. After this process the wafers had polySilicon
deposited for 3 hours and 35 minutes, which resulted in a layer of approximately 2 µm measured
on the ellipsometer. This is a little more than the designed 1.8 µm, and should thus raise the

The ellipsometer did not seem very accurate in its high
thickness polySilicon measuring programs, and the 2µm was only taken as an estimate. Afterwards
phosphorous was pre-deposited using a POCl3 gas at 1000 ◦C, to reach a resistivity of 6 Ωcm,
measured using a 4-point probe. The wafers were dipped in BHF to remove the oxide formed
during pre-deposition. No Drive-In step was made, meaning that the phosphorous ion distribution
throughout the polySilicon wafer was maybe non-ideal.
The wafers were polished in a Chemical-Mechanical Polishing (CMP) system, which uses a
slurry with silica particles and mechanical pressure applied by two rotating platforms, to polish
wafers. The wafers sits in a recess in one of the two rotating platforms, with variable height, so
that the wafer protrudes more or less out of the recess. With the height adjustments necessary, the
wafers had a thickness causing them to either stick out too much, named H, or too little, named
L. These will later be used to name the bonded wafers as well. Some runs were made with both
of the heights, where both methods seemed to result in a ﬁne polishing visually. After the CMP,
the wafers were post CMP cleaned using a brush and megasonic water to remove residual slurry in
a machine speciﬁcally designed for this. Though the process should clean the wafer, some results
from the MEMS group have shown that it might actually make the wafers dirtier. Nanolab has
found no signiﬁcant increase in particles after taking a new wafer directly through the cleaner.
Due to mixed results, the wafers were cleaned using the post CMP cleaner.
Another cleaning step was required to further clean the wafers, as the post CMP clean is not
enough to remove particles completely. The MEMS group has had continuous issues with particles
introduced from the CMP, that inhibited bonding. A slightly new cleaning procedure was thus
tested, consisting of 3 steps. The ﬁrst is a ’rough’ cleaning in one beaker using a piranha solution
with a 3:1 mixture of 98 % H2SO4 and 30 % H2O2, being very similar in nature to the 7-up.
The second step involves a BHF dip, as the silica particles might have oxidized unto the wafer,
and the BHF should remove this adsorption. During the BHF step the wafers were stirred gently.
Furthermore, the Piranha solution might have oxidized the wafer further, potentially shovelling an
oxide beneath particles to help them being lifted oﬀ. The last step is another 3:1 piranha solution
in a new beaker for ’ﬁne’ cleaning. The wafers were rinsed in DI-water after the steps. For this
cleaning, they were washed rigorously under a DI-water spray gun for a couple of minutes. The
submerging time was 10 minutes, 5 minutes and 10 minutes in the corresponding solutions.
The roughness was measured on a PSOI L and a PSOI before polishing, and the results are
displayed in Table 7.4, where a huge improvement in surface RMS roughness can be seen after
polishing. Most parameters have decreased an order of magnitude, and the RMS roughness is at
a point where it can spontaneously bond. The scans were done at the same scan range.
The AFM scan before polishing the PSOI can be seen in Figure 7.24a where the individual
grains of the polySilicon can be seen. After polishing the surface has a wavy-wormy look as shown
in Figure 7.24b, and is much ﬂatter than before.
7.6
Bonding and thinning of PSOI wafer
2 substrate wafers and 2 PSOI wafers, one polished with high protrusion, H, and one with a low,
L, were RCA1 cleaned and rinsed in DI water as this step had resulted in the least amount of
particles after the RCA particle test. The wafers were then dried in a spin dryer, and without
exiting the laminar ﬂow of air under a HEPA ﬁlter the wafers were hand-bonded. They were not
pressed together during the bond, but were only bonded gently. This resulted in PSOI H and the
==PAGE:120==
7.6. BONDING AND THINNING OF PSOI WAFER
115
(a) 5 µm by 5 µm scan of a PSOI wafer showing
the polySilicon grains.
(b) 0.5 µm by 0.5 µm after polishing showing the
wavy-wormy surface after a polish.
Figure 7.24: AFM scan before and after polishing the PSOI. These scans were done with diﬀerent
ranges.
substrate wafer to slide a little and then bond when loaded in a carrier. These wafers were broken
apart and bonded again. The hand-bonded wafers were then bonded further in a wafer bonding
machine, that heats up the wafers and applies pressure under vacuum. To further improve the
bond and establish covalent bonding across the wafers, the 2 bonded pairs L and H, were annealed
at 1100 ◦C for 12 hours.
To check the bond quality at this point, a PhotoLuminescense (PL) Map can be done. This
shines light with a long wavelength at the wafer, which penetrate the wafer, and at surface bound-
aries are reﬂected. This is usually done through the PSOI wafer as it is thin and polished on
both sides meaning light penetrates easily. Due to the wafer backside being too rough and the
wafer being too thick, better results were gotten by shining light through the substrate wafer. A
good rule of thumb is; the more deﬁned the structured regions are, the better the bond is. Figure
7.26 and 7.25 shows the PL-maps for Wafer H and Wafer L. Wafer L shows a larger unbonded
surface around the perimeter of the wafer, probably due to being split open and rebonded. Wafer
H shows more deﬁned regions of the array structures, and is thus more likely to be a better bond.
Interestingly, both wafers show an unbonded region around the alignment marks to the left, and
for Wafer L, also to the right. Recalling from Chapter 4, some of the alignment marks resulted in
regions being oxidized twice during the double LOCOS - ﬁrst making a bump, and then further
oxidizing the bump. This would result in a structure 1-2 µm higher than the rest of the post oxide,
which has probably resulted in unbonded regions around the alignment marks. This can actually
be a good thing, since if this region is removed, aligning would be easier in the coming processing.
After PL-mapping the wafers were ﬁrst dipped in polySilicon etch solution for a total of 47
minutes to remove the polySilicon deposited on the backside of the PSOI wafer. Afterwards they
were submerged in BHF for 5 minutes to remove the 400 nm box oxide layer of the PSOI, but
which also removed some oxide on the back of the substrate wafer. The hydrophillic/phobic test
was made to check if the layers were etched as they should.
Then the Silicon handle layer was thinned down using a dry etch with an SF6 cycled with
C4F8 chemistry in the etching machine called ASE, which is primarily used for Silicon etching.
The ﬁeld generating the plasma, and the ﬁeld accelerating ions unto the wafer, are not entirely
uniform. Longer etches then will create bigger variations across the wafer during the etch. The
etch is faster, 7-8 µm/min, on the perimeter of the wafer, decreasing towards the center. After 60
minutes the wafers were kept under constant supervision. Around the 70 minute mark, the oxide
begins to become visible at the border of the wafer, where the perimeter of the wafer begins to
change to a variety of colors. The wafers should then be removed, as subsequent etch poses a risk
of the remaining bonded wafer to be ripped oﬀdue to e.g. stresses. This might tear oﬀregions that
were otherwise well-bonded. In Figure 7.27 the wafer after thinning down in the ASE is shown.
The badly bonded region shown in Figure 7.25 broke oﬀduring the dry etch processing,
==PAGE:121==
116
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION - 2ND BATCH
Figure 7.25: Pl-map of bonding of Wafer L.
The rest of the thinning was done in the more gentle KOH solution for 50-60 minutes. The
solution also etches the oxide, so the process should be stopped when the etching has visibly
stopped. The wafer ﬁzzes in regions where it is being etched, and when the ﬁzzing stops, the wafer
is ready to be pulled out of the solution. As the etch rate is a little over 1 µm/min the uniformity
of the ASE etch across the wafer, was deemed to be within 10-15 %. For both wafer H and L, the
badly bonded regions were now removed. after the handle-layer was removed, the box oxide was
removed in BHF. It was at this point hard to gauge, whether the oxide had been removed and the
wafers were subjected to the BHF for a total of 15 minutes after which it passed the hydrophillic
test. This resulted in a top-plate of polySilicon around 2 µm in thickness. During the KOH etch
some additional regions were opened up and etched, and the visual result is shown in Figure 7.28
where the resultant etched region correspond quite well with the badly bonded regions predicted
by the PL-map.
7.7
Deﬁnition of the bottom and top electrode
A 2 µm thick layer of AZ MiR was then spin coated on the wafers with an HMDS priming step.
As only a few arrays in the original mask set was designed for top plate separation, the ’etch-
to-bottom’ mask and ’aluminum etch’ mask was redesigned. It takes a couple of weeks for new
masks to arrive from the producer, so it was decided to do the alignment and exposure in the new
Maskless Aligner (MLA) tool. This machine aligns points in the digital mask ﬁle, to the points
on the actual substrate. It is also able to use 4-point alignment, so that any Scaling/Skewing
of the mask from the producer, or during processing can be accommodated. Scaling is a simple
k ·(xdesign, ydesign) = (xactual, yactual) relation , where k is a constant and (xdesign, ydesign) are the
coordinates of the mask design and (xactual, yactual) are the actual point on the substrate.
In Chapter 4 Figure 4.8 showed the design for the plate separation, which was now replicated
==PAGE:122==
7.7. DEFINITION OF THE BOTTOM AND TOP ELECTRODE
117
Figure 7.26: PL-map of bonding of Wafer H.
Figure 7.27: Bonded pair of wafers after thinning the handle layer in the ASE dry etch machine.
on all arrays for the ’etch-to-bottom’ mask and ’aluminum etch’ mask. This was done by inverting
the ’DREM’ mask into the ’aluminum etch’ mask and adding large rectangles for bottom contacts
on both masks.
As the ’etch-to-bottom’ mask only consisted of large rectangles some hundreds of microns from
==PAGE:123==
118
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION - 2ND BATCH
(a) Wafer H.
(b) Wafer H.
Figure 7.28: Bonded wafers after KOH etch of handle layer.
the array, it was not very sensitive to alignment errors on scaling, and thus 4-point alignment was
not used. This resulted in an alignment that was almost perfect on the left side of the wafer but a
little over 2.2 µm (gauged by the Vernier marks) misaligned on the right side as shown in Figure
7.29. The 4-point alignment was done for wafer L resulting in a scaling constant of 1.0003, meaning
that over the full length of the mask, the pattern was 3 µm misaligned. This shows the power of
the Vernier marks, and if more lines had been used, it would probably have captured this error
more precisely, than for the number of lines used on this mask.
(a) Left alignment marks showing good alignment.
(b) Right alignment marks where the Vernier marks
show that there is above 2.2 µm misalignment.
Figure 7.29: Alignment marks after exposure and developmen of the Etch-to-Bottom mask.
After the alignment and exposure, the wafers were developed using the procedure described
above.
First, the top plate had to be etched in the regions where the opening to the bottom
substrate was deﬁned.
This was done using the Pegasus dry etching machine, with a Bosch
cycling process. The recipe used for top plate etching had a decreased platen power compared
to a standard Bosch process, to avoid ’Necking’, which is an underetch at the Si/SiO2 interface
due to reﬂective sputtering of radicals. The wafers were then subjective to a dry SiO2 etch using
the AOE as described in Chapter 6. The wafers were etched for 16 minutes, which should have
resulted in the 3 µm oxide being completely etched. A visual check was made and height was
proﬁled. These inspections showed signs the post oxide had been completely removed. However at
a closer inspection,some resistance measurements and by discussion with the MEMS group, it was
decided that the oxide was not completely removed. The wafers were then processed 4 minutes
==PAGE:124==
7.7. DEFINITION OF THE BOTTOM AND TOP ELECTRODE
119
more in the AOE. The bottom of the trenches now had a clear grey color indicating it was Silicon
and that the oxide had been removed. The resist was then stripped using the standard plasma
ashing recipe. Afterwards, both wafers had 20 nm Ti at a deposition rate of 5˚A/s and subsequent
400 nm Al at a deposition rate of 10 ˚A/s deposited using an Electron-beam evaporator machine.
This machine has line-of-sight deposition, which is enabled by high vacuum, and deposits very
pure layers of material, by locally heating small pockets of material with an E-beam source. The
Titanium functions as an adhesion layer for the Aluminum.
As the only Silicon dry etcher, in which aluminum was allowed to enter, the ASE, was not
usable at the time, some process optimisation needed to be done to etch the top plate. Another
dry etcher, called ’ICP Metal’ which was not optimised for silicon was used instead. The machine
was also meant to be used for 6” wafers and not 4”, which were used in this project. This meant
that the wafer could not be cooled directly, which was necessary for some resists. Otherwise the
resist might char during processing. This could be bypassed by using an adhesive called ’crystal
bond’. Thus a 4” wafer could be mounted on a 6” wafer in which the adhesive serves as a heat
bridge. This was a little cumbersome to use, and so it was decided to process the wafer without
it. This would also eliminate the steps of applying and removing the crystal bond.
With the help of the nanolab employee, Berit Herstrøm, a recipe was incorporated from the
ASE into the ICP Metal to etch Silicon. The process utilised only 100 W platen power, and no coil
power to generate the plasma. The coil power generates a high density plasma, which increases
the temperature compared to a lower density. The ﬂow rates of the gases were 40 sccm SF6 and
27 sccm O2. The combination of low power, and low ﬂow rate would make the etch rate decrease.
This was counteracted by setting the pressure to 80 mTorr, which is a relatively high pressure.
This creates a denser plasma, but with temperatures lower than if the coil power was used. From
a trial run, the etch rate in Silicon was shown to be around 400 nm/min, meaning a 5 minute etch
should remove the top plate in the desired regions. The post oxide underneath the polySilicon on
the bonded wafers should be able to withstand a slight over etch. Thus a test run was also made
where oxide was etched for 10 minutes. This resulted in 220 nm oxide etched, giving an etch rate
of 22nm/min. Both AZ MiR 701 and AZ nLOF was then etched for 10 minutes each. They were
not burned/charred, and there was still resist left after processing.
It was then decided that the Aluminum and Titanium etch could potentially be incorporated
using a resist mask and in a single process step along with the polySilicon. Thus AZ MiR 701 and
nLOF 2020 was tested using the aluminum etch recipe in the metal etcher. The MiR was tested
ﬁrst and showed visible signs of charring. It was then decided to test nLOF, but with a lower coil
and platen power. After around 50 minutes etch at increasingly higher power the nLOF still did
not char, but the resist was visibly etched at this point. It was then decided to try the normal
coil and platen power for a new layer of nLOF resist, which withstood 4 minutes, before being
etched/charred.
The resist thickness should be 4 µm (instead of 1.5 or 2 µm) as the trenches etched to the
bottom substrate were 5 µm deep. So nLOF with a resist thickness of 4 µm was spin-coated on
wafer H, L, a PSOI test wafer and a couple of dummy wafers with a Ti/Al layer.
The exposure of the nLOF resist was then optimized enough so that the it could be used as a
mask for the ICP metal dry etcher. The optimization was done in the MLA. The MLA has two
parameters aﬀecting exposure; Defocus and Dose. The machine has the ability to design a matrix
of diﬀerent defoci and doses. Defocus aﬀects where the light is focused in the z-plane. By checking
the diﬀerent defocus/doses in a microscope, it was found that an opening around 2.5 was achieved
for nLOF with light having a wavelength of 375 nm wavelength, a light dose of 400 mJ/cm2 and
defocus with a value of -9. As nLOF is a negative resist it has sidewalls that are negative. For
small openings of a few microns, this can enlarge the opening at the bottom of the resist by 2-3
times, which is undesired. Thus, instead of 60 seconds post exposure bake, 120 seconds was used,
to decrease the slope of the sidewalls. The wafers were then puddle developed for 60 seconds, and
the dummy wafers were cleaved and cross-sectional inspected in a microscope. Here it could be
seen, that not all of the resist had been removed at the bottom and so the wafers were developed
an additional 90 seconds.
First the PSOI wafer was tested in the ICP Metal and it was found that the polySilicon had
been etched after around 6 minutes. Then a test with a Ti/Al covered dummy was then subjected
to a dry aluminum etch at 500 W coil and 100 W platen for 2 minutes using 25 sccm Cl2 and 15
==PAGE:125==
120
CHAPTER 7. FABRICATION - 2ND BATCH
sccm HBr which is the standard Al etch recipe. It was found to etch the Al layer, and inspection
in the microscope showed that the Ti had not been removed. The Ti layer was then subjected to
the lower coil/platen (250/80 W) power Al etch recipe for 8 minutes, and inspection showed that
the Ti had been removed. The Ti is also etched by a Chlorine based etch, and so the Aluminum
etch can work, though not as well as a more Ti optimized etch. The machine could however not
sustain the power required by the Ti etch recipe at the moment of processing.
The wafer WL was then subjected to the polySilicon etch for 6 minutes, the standard Al etch
for 2 minutes and lastly the low power Al etch for 8 minutes to separate top plate elements. After
processing, the elements had started to delaminate as shown in Figure 7.30. The delamination
seems to start at the edge of the elements. Some reasons could be; A potential under etch beneath
the top plate. The bonding quality was maybe not good enough after all. Stress in the top plate
also be a cause of the problem. The test arrays were not aﬀected by this problem. Thus it seems,
that if a very safe design had been employed this delamination problem would not have been
encountered. This exempliﬁes the very important trade-oﬀdiscussed earlier between opting for a
safe more experimentally isolated design and and a more risky design that is closer to the end goal.
There was no discernible diﬀerence between arrays with more bonding area compared to the ones
with less.
Figure 7.30: Wafer WL after the top plate separation. The elements have started to delaminate.
The wafer was inspected visually, and only 3 out of 27 arrays seemed fully functional with
no delaminated or missing top plate. 24 out of the 27 arrays had at least one intact element (in
general more). Finally the wafer was spin-coated with 2 layers of 4 µm thick protective resist and
taken out of the cleanroom to be diced.
Lastly some of the designed and measured process parameters are show in Table 7.5
7.8
Summary
In this chapter, the processing of the second batch of wafers using the conventional double LOCOS
without a DREM trench with ﬁlling was presented. An emphasis was put into documentation of

the process step by step to minimize processing errors. A wafer with 3 fully intact arrays, and
21 arrays with missing elements was fabricated, giving a very high yield in terms of electrical

Chapter 8
Electrical characterization
In this chapter three diﬀerent arrays will be electrically characterized. The arrays are L4, M5,
and M2 corresponding to radii of R=19.5 µm, 22.5 µm and 31 µm respectively. As the discussion
on the characterization will be ﬂowing back and forth between the diﬀerent arrays, an overview
of the calculated and measured parameters will be given here in the beginning. This is displayed
in Table 8.1 to 8.3. The calculated values for, CT , the total electrical capacitance and, CP , the
parasitic capacitance are estimated using a parallel plate capacitor model placed in parallel in a
circuit. The thicknesses of the diﬀerent layers as well as the capacitors areas are based on the
design. The resonance frequencies were derived in Chapter 2 along with the coupling coeﬃcient,
k and the pull-in potential VP I. VP I,sim is the pull-in potential estimated by a COMSOL model
made in the MEMS group. COMSOL is a Finite EleMent software well suited for multiphysics.
Without going too much in depth, the COMSOl takes into account, the eﬀective electrode area of
the bump as well as the aluminum on the top plate. The thickness of the top plate was 2 µm in
all calculations. Imax is the theoretical maximum value of the leakage current through the oxide
using the total area of the array and a resistivity of 1014Ωcm. The corrected resonance frequencies
have taken the increased radius into account.

wafer prober which can perform electrical characterization on wafer scale. The three arrays were
I-V, Z-f, C-f and C-V characterized as shown in Figure 8.1 for element 89 on array L4. One probe
acted as the ground terminal and was placed on the substrate electrode. The substrate electrode
was the one made using the ’etch to bottom’ mask. Another probe acted as AC/DC source and

IV measurements from LinArray
Figure 8.2: Showing the max current measured on all the elements of array L4. The blue color

was placed on a single element completing the circuit. The overview in Figure 8.1 shows the gen-
eral trend of behaviour in elements that worked ﬁne. Starting from the top, the Current-Voltage
curve is seen to increase with increasing potential in a slightly non-linear behaviour. Moving one
plot down, the magnitude of the impedance is plotted as a function of frequency in a log-scale
plot. The impedance magnitude of a capacitor has the form
1
fC , a linear decreasing function on a
log-scale. This is what is seen and thus the system works like a capacitor as expected. The phase
change in the middle is caused by the electronics in the probe station. The third plot shows the
capacitance as a function of the frequency. The capacitance should be constant with increasing
frequency, which also seem to be the case here. The dip is caused by the electronics. The phase
should be -90◦for a capacitor which can also be seen on the plots. In these plots a small DC bias is
applied, and a small AC signal appended on top. The bottom plot shows capacitance as a function
of DC potential bias. The DC bias is varied from 0 V to 100 V, then down to -100 V and back
to 0V again. The electromechanical system has a parabolic C-V behaviour which is also seen on
the plot. The parabolic behaviour comes from the fact that as DC bias is applied, the top plate is
pulled down, increasing the capacitance. These plots also reveals electric charging, which shows as
hysteresis. There seem to be some charging as the circles are not entirely on top of one another.
These measurements were made on each element on an array. In Figure 8.2 the maximum
current for every element is plotted showing that few are short-circuited (SC) indicated by a red
color. This is an oxide-shorting and should not be confused with inter-element shorting. The limit
for SC is set to 1 nA, which might not be an actual short-circuit, though it is a signiﬁcant decrease
in performance. The current can be seen to vary a little from element to element and this might
be due to small diﬀerences in oxide/nitride quality and thickness as well as the actual area of the

CV measurements from LinArray
Figure 8.3: Showing the max capacitance for a DC bias of 100 V measured on all the elements of
array L4. The blue color corresponds to working elements, and the red corresponds to non-working

top electrode. The average value of the max current measured at 100V DC bias is on the same
order as the calculated one. The resistivity of the oxide/nitride is set to the lower limit found in
[16]. This is the case for two of the array arrays, with M2 having a measured Imax an order of
magnitude higher than the other two.
Figure 8.3 shows the maximum capacitance measured on all the elements. For most of the
arrays, the maximum capacitance is the same. This is actually also the case for the oxide-shorting,
meaning that they are not quite short-circuited but just have a higher leakage current. A few of
the elements stand out with a higher capacitance. This is very likely due to an actual inter-element
SC. By closer inspection, the bars the capacitance increase happens in clusters of 2-4 elements.
As inter-element SC results in two capacitors connected in parallel, the capacitance should then
also be 2-4 times as high. This can also be seen to be the case for some of the clusters, where
the increases are in integer steps depending on the amount of elements in the cluster. Thus a CV
measurement on a linear array reveals inter-element SC. Some are also lower, which could indicate
damaged CMUT structures. One is very low and is given the color green to indicate that it has
not been contacted properly.
The capacitance at 0 bias was also found for all three arrays. For M5 and L4 they corresponded
very well with the calculated value, in which the relative deviation being within a couple of percent.
This suggests, that the parasitic capacitance calculated, is also very close to the actual value.
However, as both CP and C0 is part of CT , to be able to truly test it, test structures should be
incorporated on the mask design. The parasitic capacitance is 2 orders of magnitude lower than
==PAGE:132==
8.2. Z-F MEASUREMENTS FOR RESONANCE FREQUENCY MEASUREMENT
127
the total electrical capacitance CT . As parasitic capacitance contributes to noise in the system,
this is a very nice property of this CMUT design. For M2 this was not the case and there was
a relative deviation of 20.77 % so the same cannot be concluded for this array. This array then
might have an increased parasitic capacitance.
For array M5 and M2 the probes were accidentally reversed for half of the elements, which
resulted in erroneous results for half of the elements. The results are shown in Appendix B. The
rest of the elements were used to ﬁnd the values presented in Table 8.1 and 8.3.
8.2
Z-f measurements for resonance frequency measurement
By changing probes in the setup, the frequency can be swept across even larger ranges. Before a
large frequency sweep, the probes were calibrated to a phase shift of -90◦and an impedance of 49
kΩ. For M5 and L4 the bias was varied from -10 V to -240 V, and for M2 -10 V to -100 in steps
of 10 V. The frequency was swept for every bias from 1-20 MHz. M2 reached pull-in somewhere
below -100 V.
The arrays were observed to charge over time. Charging is a problem that occurs in the capacitors,
when there are charge states in the system that can counteract the potential applied across the
capacitor. The charging seemed to be less present for negative voltages which is why only negative
sweeps has been made. The charging could be due to particles in the nitride ﬁlm or that KOH was
used to strip polySilicon.
The DC bias is turned oﬀbetween sweeping steps to reduce charging.
Figure 8.4 shows the
impedance magnitude as a function of frequency for diﬀerent biases for an element array M5.
From the ﬁgure can be seen that as the bias is increased, minima and maxima peaks become
more deﬁned and larger in amplitude. For small biases they are almost non-existent. Comparing
to Figure 2.4 the minima correspond to the resonance frequency and the maxima correspond to
the anti-resonance frequency. The resonance peaks shift to lower frequencies with increased bias,
which is expected, due to the spring softening eﬀect. The measured resonance frequency were read
oﬀof the plot at the lowest bias in which the peaks were visible. The resonance frequency was
16, 12.5 and 7.1 MHz for R = 19.5, 22,5 and 31 µm respectively. The relative deviation to the
(corrected) predicted value was 19.1, 17.2 and 7.8 %. The radius is a bit higher for M2 than for
the other two. This pattern in the relative deviation is the same pattern as was seen in Chapter
3. Here the TABLA VI probe had a lower relative deviation in measured and predicted resonance
frequency. It could be interesting to look more into this pattern. The resonance frequencies are a
bit lower than the predicted values. The predicted values were derived based on a lossless system,
which this is not entirely, and so there is expected to be some discrepancy. They do decrease with
increasing radius as expected. The measured (anti)resonance frequency used for calculating the
coupling constant was read oﬀof the graphs at maximum bias. These are shown in Figure 8.6 and
8.7. The coupling coeﬃcient is found to be 16.7, 18.7 and 8.5 % for R = 19.5, 22,5 and 31 µm
respectively. The lower coupling constant for R = 31 µm might be due to an increase in parasitic
capacitance. However they are diﬃcult to compare exactly, as the exact pull-in potential was not
found and as the coupling coeﬃcient is dependent on the value VDC
VP I .
Figure 8.5 shows the phase shift between potential and current as a function of frequency for
diﬀerent bias potentials for an element in array M5. For low biases the phase shift is close to
-90◦for all frequencies, which is expected. As the bias increases a peak in the phase shift begins to
form. This peak grows in magnitude and shifts to the left as expected. For large biases it seems
to level oﬀ, meaning the system is close to pull-in. The phase shift peak is around 30 ◦which is
quite far from the ideal 180 ◦. Figure 8.6 and 8.7 also shows the phase shift, and it is read oﬀof
the graphs to be 15 and 18 for R = 19.5 and 31 µm respectively. These values are even further
from the ideal values. This might be due to insuﬃcient resolution in frequency steps and further
experiments could maybe resolve this issue. Another important thing to keep in mind is that there
is some distribution of the gap heights, plate thickness and cavity radius over the wafer and over
the single elements. This means they do not all have the same exact resonance frequency, and this
will decrease the peak of the phase shift and widen its shape.

8.3
Summary
Electrical characterization using I-V, C-V, C-f and Z-f measurement techniques was performed
on array L4, M5 and M2 corresponding to R = 19.5, 22.5 and 31 µm.
They were shown to
work as capacitors as expected and had the expected electromechanical behaviour. The resonance
frequency in air was determined to be 16, 12.5 and 7.1 MHz for R = 19.5, 22,5 and 31 µm
respectively. The relative deviation to the predicted value was 19.1, 17.2 and 7.8 %. The coupling
coeﬃcient was found to be 16.7, 18.7 and 8.5 % respectively.
It was deemed likely that they
had very low parasitic capacitance, but further testing was needed to verify this. The arrays also
showed charging behaviour which could be due to particles in the nitride ﬁlm and a polySilicon
strip in KOH.

==PAGE:137==
Chapter 9
Conclusion and Outlook
In this thesis initial process and design development was carried out towards the inclusion of a
ﬁlled deep narrow etched trench in a double LOCal Oxidation of Silicon (LOCOS) process to
fabricate linear CMUT arrays for laparoscopy. CMUT arrays with 192 elements utilizing a double
LOCOS process were fabricated. The cells had a high Si-bump height and a thick post-oxide,
and a substrate wafer was fusion bonded successfully to a polySilicon-On-Insulator wafer made in-
house. The arrays were electrically characterized using an automated setup. The predicted total
capacitance of the arrays, were within a couple of percent of the measured value. The predicted
low parasitic capacitance was thus deemed likely as well. This further motivates the inclusion of
an oxide ﬁlled DREM etched trench for CMUT’s since it improves the performance of the device.
Analyzing the results of novel OnScale simulations made by Post Doc Mathias Engholm, the
design space of the CMUT was discussed. The analysis clearly showed a crucial Bandwidth/Sen-
sitivity performance trade-oﬀintrinsic for CMUT’s. By evaluating this trade-oﬀ, cell radii of 19.5
µm, 22.5 µm and 31 µm, and a plate thickness of 1.8 µm were chosen for the arrays so that they
would perform within the desired speciﬁcations. This was implemented in a new mask design. two
diﬀerent element pitches were used, which were 0.5λ and 0.7λ where λ is the wavelength of a 7.5
MHz wave in water. In the mask design, distance from cell perimeter to trench was varied to test
diﬀerent degrees of bonding area available.
A full process integration of the ﬁlled DREM etch was tried out in one batch of Silicon wafers.
Two essential problems was encountered in this batch. The ﬁrst one was that the usual spin-coating
process did not work due to the increased height of the Si-bumps. This problem was solved by
increasing the thickness of the spin-coated photoresist.
The second problem was that a large
wafer bow was introduced during the 2nd LOCOS process. This problem was characterized, which
showed that the oxide had grown more inside the trench than expected/possible. The expected
value was 5 µm and they oxide grown was 6 µm. Several solutions to the the problem was proposed.
One was to decrease the oxidation time to so the trench would not be overﬁlled. Another was to
introduce both macro- and local scale openings that would not get ﬁlled to relieve some of the
stresses introduced.
Another batch was initiated in which a more thorough documentation was the focus to better
catch potential error sources. In this batch, an increased Si-bump height and thick post oxide
was implemented. One of the wafers were fusion bonded to a polished PSOI wafer. A particle
problem was also circumvented by implementing a new cleaning procedure suggested by Post Doc
Andreas Spandet Havreland. A new top plate etching process was also shown to work, though not
optimally. Al, Ti and polySilicon was dry etched consecutively without the use of crystal bond
and with photoresist as masking material. A wafer with 3 fully intact arrays, and 21 arrays with
missing elements was fabricated, giving a very high yield in terms of electrical characterization.
Three arrays, each with a diﬀerent cell radius was characterized. They were shown to behave
as intended, showing capacitive electrical behaviour and small leakage current.
The expected
electromechanical behavior was also veriﬁed. It was shown that the resonance frequency decreased
with increasing cell radius, and that increasing the bias also decreased the resonance frequency
due to spring softening as expected. The resonance frequency in air was determined to be 16, 12.5
and 7.1 MHz for R = 19.5, 22,5 and 31 µm respectively. The relative deviation to the predicted
132
==PAGE:138==
133
value was 19.1, 17.2 and 7.8 %. The coupling coeﬃcient was found to be 16.7, 18.7 and 8.5 %
respectively. However the arrays also charged, and the signal would decrease over time if subjected
to continuous DC bias. This might have been due to surface states introduced by a nitride ﬁlm
with too many particles, or the implementation of a KOH strip of polySilicon during processing.
For future projects, several studies could be interesting. Firstly, the test arrays could be mounted
on Printed Circuit Boards to test them acoustically. Thus further comparisons to the OnScale
simulations. Either to further verify the validity of the simulation results, or to improve upon the
model. It would also show if the CMUT had the performance intended in terms of bandwidth
and sensitivity. It could also clarify why the simulations seem to perform worse for lower values
of radii. A test with diﬀerent plate thicknesses to further compare with simulated results and
expand the known parameter space could also be an interesting study. In regards to the process
development, some test could be done on trench density and oxidation time to see how much this
aﬀects wafer bow. A study of diﬀerent stress-relief structures both using diﬀerent mask designs but
also diﬀerent etch techniques could also help to solve the stress related problem. Longer into the
future oxidation time and temperature could be further investigated to see if this made it possible
to increase the available bonding area, without decreasing the performance. Other trench ﬁlling
methods would also be interesting to try and implement such as depositing a TEOS oxide, instead
of growing a thermal oxide. More process optimization could be done on the Chemical Mechanical
polishing. This could also be a promising method to increase the bonding area. It has already
shown it is able to polish a rough polySilicon surface, to a suﬃciently ﬂat surface for bonding.

How to use this template (works only with the .dotx template file): 
• 
Fill out the fields in the heading! 
• 
Add process steps in the following way:  
1. Select a process step header and one or more detail steps. Make sure to select the whole line so that the 
marking extends beyond the table to the right. 
 
2. Press <crtl> C to copy the part. 
3. Select the step header where you want to insert the new step. Again make sure to select the whole line. 
4. Press <ctrl> V to insert to new step. 
• 
The Content (TOC) on the last page is an option, but can give a nice overview for very long process flows.

Objective 
Batch name: Error! Reference source not found. 
This is an example process flow to be used as a template. It should contain 
• 
The objective of the process.  
• 
Substrates/samples used in the flow. Both actual samples to be processed (device wafers) and monitor samples 
for the different process steps 
• 
The Process flow main processes and steps 
• 
Recommended: Figures illustrating the sample before and after each main process step. 
How to use this template (works only with the .dotx template file): 
• 
Fill out the fields in the heading! 
• 
Add process steps in the following way:  
1. Select a process step header and one or more detail steps. Make sure to select the whole line so that the 
marking extends beyond the table to the right. 
 
2. Press <crtl> C to copy the part. 
3. Select the step header where you want to insert the new step. Again make sure to select the whole line. 
4. Press <ctrl> V to insert to new step. 
• 
The Content (TOC) on the last page is an option, but can give a nice overview for very long process flows.
